846 <section id="_1">

847

848 <h2>

849General Information About Retinoblastoma</h2>

850

851 <div id="_section_1" class="pdq-sections">

852<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

853<h6>

854In This Section</h6>

855<ul>

856<li>

857<a href="#_486_toc">

858Incidence</a>

859</li>

860<li>

861<a href="#_716_toc">

862Anatomy</a>

863</li>

864<li>

865<a href="#_722_toc">

866Screening</a>

867</li>

868<li>

869<a href="#_718_toc">

870Clinical Presentation</a>

871</li>

872<li>

873<a href="#_720_toc">

874Diagnostic and Staging Evaluation</a>

875</li>

876<li>

877<a href="#_488_toc">

878Genetics and Genomics of Retinoblastoma<ul>

879<li>

880<a href="#_sm_CDR0000779398_6_toc">

881Genomics of Retinoblastoma</a>

882</li>

883</ul>

884</a>

885</li>

886<li>

887<a href="#_646_toc">

888Genetic Testing</a>

889</li>

890<li>

891<a href="#_790_toc">

892Genetic Counseling</a>

893</li>

894<li>

895<a href="#_528_toc">

896Postdiagnosis Surveillance</a>

897</li>

898<li>

899<a href="#_493_toc">

900Causes of Retinoblastoma-Related Mortality<ul>

901<li>

902<a href="#_496_toc">

903Trilateral retinoblastoma</a>

904</li>

905</ul>

906</a>

907</li>

908</ul>

909</nav>

910<div class="pdq-hp-patient-toggle">

911<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/patient/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq">

912Go to Patient Version</a>

913</div>

914<p id="_729" tabindex="-1">

915Retinoblastoma is a pediatric cancer that requires careful integration of multidisciplinary care. Treatment of retinoblastoma aims to save the patient's life and preserve useful vision. For patients presenting with extraocular retinoblastoma, treatment with systemic chemotherapy and radiation therapy is likely to be curative. However, extraorbital disease requires intensive chemotherapy and may include consolidation with high-dose chemotherapy and autologous hematopoietic stem cell rescue with or without radiation therapy. While a large proportion of patients with systemic extra&#8211;central nervous system (CNS) metastases can be cured, the prognosis for patients with intracranial disease is dismal.</p>

916<section id="_486">

917<h3 id="_486_toc">

918Incidence</h3>

919<p id="_487" tabindex="-1">

920Retinoblastoma is a relatively uncommon tumor of childhood that arises in the

921retina and accounts for about 3% of the cancers occurring in children younger

922than 15 years. </p>

923<p id="_730" tabindex="-1">

924Retinoblastoma is a cancer of the very young child; two-thirds of all cases of retinoblastoma are diagnosed before age 2 years.[<a href="#cit/section_1.1">

9251</a>

926] Thus, while the estimated annual incidence in the United States is approximately 4 cases per 1 million children younger than 15 years, the age-adjusted annual incidence in children aged 0 to 4 years is 10 to 14 cases per 1 million (approximately 1 in 14,000&#8211;18,000 live births).</p>

927</section>

928<section id="_716">

929<h3 id="_716_toc">

930Anatomy</h3>

931<p id="_717" tabindex="-1">

932Retinoblastoma arises from the retina, and it may grow under the retina and/or toward the vitreous cavity. Involvement of the ocular coats and optic nerve occurs as a sequence of events as the tumor progresses.</p>

933<p id="_1851" tabindex="-1">

934Focal invasion of the choroid is common, although massive invasion occurs in cases of advanced disease. After invading the choroid, the tumor gains access to systemic circulation and creates the potential for metastases. Further progression through the ocular coats leads to invasion of the sclera and the orbit. Tumors that invade the anterior chamber may gain access to systemic circulation through the canal of Schlemm. Progression through the optic nerve and past the lamina cribrosa increases the risk of systemic and CNS dissemination (see <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1850">

935Figure 1</a>

936). </p>

937<p id="_727" tabindex="-1">

938<figure id="figure_1850" class="image-center">

939<a href="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/798975.jpg" target="_blank" class="article-image-enlarge">

940Enlarge</a>

941<img id="_1850" alt="Anatomy of the eye; drawing shows the sclera, ciliary body, canal of Schlemm, cornea, iris, lens, vitreous humor, retina, choroid, optic nerve, and lamina cribrosa." title="Anatomy of the eye; drawing shows the sclera, ciliary body, canal of Schlemm, cornea, iris, lens, vitreous humor, retina, choroid, optic nerve, and lamina cribrosa." src="https://nci-media.cancer.gov/pdq/media/images/798975-750.jpg">

942<figcaption class="caption-container">

943Figure 1. Anatomy of the eye showing the sclera, ciliary body, canal of Schlemm, cornea, iris, lens, vitreous humor, retina, choroid, optic nerve, and lamina cribrosa. The vitreous humor is a gel that fills the center of the eye.</figcaption>

944</figure>

945</p>

946</section>

947<section id="_722">

948<h3 id="_722_toc">

949Screening</h3>

950<p id="_1838" tabindex="-1">

951Consensus reports from the American Association of Ophthalmic Oncologists and Pathologists and the American Association for Cancer Research Childhood Cancer Predisposition Workshop describe surveillance guidelines for screening children at risk of developing retinoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9522</a>

953,<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9543</a>

955]</p>

956<p id="_723" tabindex="-1">

957In children with a positive family history of retinoblastoma, early-in-life screening by fundus exam is performed under general anesthesia at regular intervals. Exams are performed according to a schedule based on the absolute estimated risk, as determined by identification of the <em class="gene-name">

958RB1</em>

959 mutation in the family and the presence of the <em class="gene-name">

960RB1</em>

961 mutation in the child.[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9622</a>

963,<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9643</a>

965]</p>

966<p id="_1852" tabindex="-1">

967Infants born to affected parents have a dilated eye examination under anesthesia as soon as possible in the first month of life, and a genetic evaluation is performed. Infants with a positive genetic test are examined under anesthesia on a monthly basis. In infants who do not develop disease, monthly exams continue throughout the first year. The frequency of those exams may be decreased progressively during the second and subsequent years. Screening children with a positive family history of retinoblastoma can improve their prognosis, in terms of globe sparing and use of less intensive, ocular-salvage treatments (see <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1881">

968Table 1</a>

969 and <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1877">

970Figure 2</a>

971).[<a href="#cit/section_1.2">

9722</a>

973,<a href="#cit/section_1.3">

9743</a>

975] </p>

976<table id="_1881" class="table-default expandable-container">

977<caption>

979RB1</em>

1113Figure 2. Management guidelines for childhood screening for <a href="https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/medicine-and-dentistry/retinoblastoma" title="https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/medicine-and-dentistry/retinoblastoma">

1114retinoblastoma</a>

1115. The presented schedules are general guidelines and reflect a schedule for examinations in which no lesions of concern are noted. It may be appropriate to examine some children more frequently. Decisions regarding examination method, examination under <a href="https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/medicine-and-dentistry/anesthesia" title="https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/medicine-and-dentistry/anesthesia">

1116anesthesia</a>

1117 (EUA) versus nonsedated examination in the office, are complex and best decided by the clinician in discussion with the patient's family. The preference of the majority of the clinical centers involved in the creation of this consensus statement is reflected, but individual centers may make policy decisions based on available resources and expert clinician preference. Examination under anesthesia will be strongly considered for any child who is unable to participate in an office examination sufficiently to allow thorough examination of the retina. *A minority of clinical centers also prefer EUA for high- and intermediate-risk children (calculated risk &gt;1%) from birth to 8 weeks of age. Reprinted from <a href="https://www.sciencedirect.com/journal/ophthalmology" title="https://www.sciencedirect.com/journal/ophthalmology">

1118Ophthalmology</a>

1119, Volume 125, Issue 3, Alison H. Skalet, Dan S. Gombos, Brenda L. Gallie, Jonathan W. Kim, Carol L. Shields, Brian P. Marr, Sharon E. Plon, Patricia Ch&#233;vez-Barrios, Screening Children at Risk for Retinoblastoma: Consensus Report from the American Association of Ophthalmic Oncologists and Pathologists, Pages 453&#8211;458, Copyright (2018), with permission from Elsevier.</figcaption>

1120</figure>

1121<p id="_724" tabindex="-1">

1122It is common practice to use ophthalmic examinations to screen the parents and siblings of patients with retinoblastoma to exclude an unknown familial disease. However, in the absence of genetic testing, the screening plan for a child with a biological parent who had unilateral retinoblastoma is not well defined.[<a href="#cit/section_1.4">

11234</a>

1124] </p>

1125</section>

1126<section id="_718">

1127<h3 id="_718_toc">

1128Clinical Presentation</h3>

1129<p id="_803" tabindex="-1">

1130Age at presentation correlates with laterality. Patients with bilateral disease present at a younger age, usually in the first 12 months of life.</p>

1131<p id="_719" tabindex="-1">

1132Most patients present with leukocoria, which is occasionally first noticed after a flash photograph is taken. Strabismus is the second most common presenting sign and usually correlates with macular involvement. Very advanced intraocular tumors present with pain, orbital cellulitis, glaucoma, or buphthalmos.</p>

1133<p id="_1868" tabindex="-1">

1134 As the tumor progresses, patients may present with orbital or metastatic disease. Metastases occur in the preauricular and laterocervical lymph nodes, in the CNS, or systemically (commonly in the bones, bone marrow, and liver).</p>

1135<p id="_787" tabindex="-1">

1136In the United States, Hispanic children and children living in lower socioeconomic conditions have presented with more advanced disease.[<a href="#cit/section_1.5">

11375</a>

1138]</p>

1139</section>

1140<section id="_720">

1141<h3 id="_720_toc">

1142Diagnostic and Staging Evaluation</h3>

1143<p id="_1853" tabindex="-1">

1144Diagnostic evaluation of retinoblastoma includes the following:</p>

1145<div class="pdq-content-list">

1146<ol id="_1854">

1147<li>

1148<strong>

1149Eye examination.</strong>

1150 Intraocular retinoblastoma is usually diagnosed without pathological confirmation. An examination under anesthesia with a maximally dilated pupil and scleral indentation is required to inspect the entire retina. A very detailed documentation of the number, location, and size of tumors; the presence of retinal detachment and subretinal fluid; and the presence of subretinal and vitreous seeds must be performed.</li>

1151<li>

1152<strong>

1153Ocular ultrasonography and magnetic resonance imaging (MRI).</strong>

1154 Bidimensional ocular ultrasonography and MRI can be useful to differentiate retinoblastoma from other causes of leukocoria and in the evaluation of extrascleral and extraocular extension in children with advanced intraocular retinoblastoma. Optic nerve enhancement by MRI does not necessarily indicate involvement. Cautious interpretation of those findings is needed.[<a href="#cit/section_1.6">

11556</a>

1156]</li>

1157</ol>

1158</div>

1159<p id="_731" tabindex="-1">

1160Patients with suspected extraocular extension by imaging or high-risk pathology in the enucleated eye (i.e., massive choroidal invasion or involvement of the sclera or the optic nerve beyond the lamina cribrosa) may need to be evaluated for the presence of metastatic disease. Patients presenting with these pathological features in the enucleated eye are at high risk of developing metastases. In these cases, the following procedures may be performed:[<a href="#cit/section_1.7">

11617</a>

1162] </p>

1163<div class="pdq-content-list">

1164<ul id="_1855">

1165<li>

1166Bone scintigraphy.</li>

1167<li>

1168Bone marrow aspiration and biopsy.</li>

1169<li>

1170Lumbar puncture.</li>

1171</ul>

1172</div>

1173</section>

1174<section id="_488">

1175<h3 id="_488_toc">

1176Genetics and Genomics of Retinoblastoma</h3>

1177<section id="_sm_CDR0000779398_6">

1178<h4 id="_sm_CDR0000779398_6_toc">

1179Genomics of Retinoblastoma</h4>

1180<p id="_sm_CDR0000779398_489" tabindex="-1">

1181Retinoblastoma is a tumor that occurs in heritable (25%&#8211;30%) and nonheritable (70%&#8211;75%) forms. Heritable disease is defined by the presence of a germline mutation of the <em class="gene-name">

1182RB1</em>

1183 gene. This germline mutation may have been inherited from an affected progenitor (25% of cases) or may have occurred in a germ cell before conception or <em>

1184in utero</em>

1185 during early embryogenesis in patients with sporadic disease (75% of cases). The presence of positive family history or bilateral or multifocal disease is suggestive of heritable disease. </p>

1186<p id="_sm_CDR0000779398_643" tabindex="-1">

1187Heritable retinoblastoma may manifest as unilateral or bilateral

1188disease. The penetrance of the <em class="gene-name">

1189RB1</em>

1190 mutation (laterality, age at diagnosis, and number of tumors) is probably dependent on concurrent genetic modifiers such as <em class="gene-name">

1191MDM2</em>

1192 and <em class="gene-name">

1193MDM4</em>

1194 polymorphisms.[<a href="#cit/section_1.8">

11958</a>

1196,<a href="#cit/section_1.9">

11979</a>

1198] All children with bilateral disease

1199 and approximately 15% of patients with unilateral disease are presumed to have the heritable form, even though only 25% have an affected parent. In a series of 482 patients with unilateral retinoblastoma, germline mutations were identified in 33% of infants younger than 12 months, 6% of children aged 12 to 24 months, and 7% of children aged 24 to 39 months. The highest incidence of germline retinoblastoma was in patients younger than 1 year compared with patients older than 1 year (odds ratio, 2.96).[<a href="#cit/section_1.10">

120010</a>

1201][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

1202Level of evidence C2</a>

1203]</p>

1204<p id="_sm_CDR0000779398_644" tabindex="-1">

1205Children with heritable retinoblastoma tend to be diagnosed at a younger age than are children with the nonheritable form of the disease.</p>

1206<p id="_sm_CDR0000779398_651" tabindex="-1">

1207Genetic counseling is recommended for all patients with retinoblastoma. For more information, see the <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_790">

1208Genetic Counseling</a>

1209 section.</p>

1210</section>

1211</section>

1212<section id="_646">

1213<h3 id="_646_toc">

1214Genetic Testing</h3>

1215<p id="_647" tabindex="-1">

1216Blood and tumor samples can be tested to determine whether a patient with retinoblastoma has a germline or somatic mutation in the <em class="gene-name">

1217RB1</em>

1218 gene. Once the patient's genetic mutation has been identified, other family members can be screened directly for the mutation with targeted sequencing. </p>

1219<p id="_648" tabindex="-1">

1220A multistep assay that includes the following may be performed for a complete genetic evaluation of the <em class="gene-name">

1221RB1</em>

1222 gene:[<a href="#cit/section_1.11">

122311</a>

1224]</p>

1225<div class="pdq-content-list">

1226<ul id="_751">

1227<li>

1228DNA sequencing to identify mutations within coding exons and immediate flanking intronic regions plus the promoter regions.</li>

1229<li>

1230Duplication/deletion analysis.</li>

1231<li>

1232Methylation analysis of the <em class="gene-name">

1233RB1</em>

1234 promoter region on DNA isolated from the tumor.</li>

1235</ul>

1236</div>

1237<p id="_752" tabindex="-1">

1238In cases of somatic mosaicism or cytogenetic abnormalities, the mutations may not be easily detected. More exhaustive techniques such as karyotyping, fluorescence <em>

1239in situ</em>

1240 hybridization, and methylation analysis of the <em class="gene-name">

1241RB1</em>

1242 promoter may be needed. Deep (2500x) sequencing of an <em class="gene-name">

1243RB1</em>

1244 genomic amplicon from lymphocyte DNA can reveal low-level mosaicism.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

124512</a>

1246] Because mosaicism is caused by a postzygotic mutation, such a finding obviates the need for serial examination of siblings under anesthesia. Current technologies will not discover some mosaic mutations at very low levels of amplification, mutations outside of the <em class="gene-name">

1247RB1</em>

1248 coding exons or the flanking intronic regions, mutations not found in lymphocytes but in other tissues (mosaic), or mosaic large rearrangements of <em class="gene-name">

1249RB1</em>

1250.[<a href="#cit/section_1.12">

125112</a>

1252] Combining the above techniques, a germline mutation may be detected in more than 90% of patients with heritable retinoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

125313</a>

1254-<a href="#cit/section_1.15">

125515</a>

1256]</p>

1257<p id="_649" tabindex="-1">

1258The absence of detectable somatic <em class="gene-name">

1259RB1</em>

1260 mutations in approximately 3% of unilateral, nonheritable retinoblastoma cases suggests that alternative genetic mechanisms may underlie the development of retinoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.16">

126116</a>

1262] In one-half of these cases, high levels of <em class="gene-name">

1263MYCN</em>

1264 amplification have been reported. These patients had distinct, aggressive histological features and a median age at diagnosis of 4 months.[<a href="#cit/section_1.17">

126517</a>

1266] However, <em class="gene-name">

1267MYCN</em>

1268 amplification has also been reported to coexist with <em class="gene-name">

1269RB1</em>

1270 mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_1.18">

127118</a>

1272] In another small subset of tumors without detectable somatic <em class="gene-name">

1273RB1</em>

1274 mutations, chromothripsis is responsible for inactivating the <em class="gene-name">

1275RB1</em>

1276 gene.[<a href="#cit/section_1.19">

127719</a>

1278]</p>

1279</section>

1280<section id="_790">

1281<h3 id="_790_toc">

1282Genetic Counseling</h3>

1283<p id="_791" tabindex="-1">

1284Genetic counseling is an integral part of the management of patients with retinoblastoma and their families, regardless of clinical presentation. Counseling includes a discussion of the main forms of retinoblastoma, which helps parents understand the genetic consequences of each form of retinoblastoma and estimate the risk of disease in family members.[<a href="#cit/section_1.13">

128513</a>

1286] Counseling also includes guidance toward appropriate screening for both patients and their families, especially if the risk of developing a second primary malignancy is increased. </p>

1287<p id="_1839" tabindex="-1">

1288Genetic counseling, however, is not always straightforward. Approximately 10% of children with retinoblastoma have somatic genetic mosaicism, which contributes to the difficulty of genetic counseling.[<a href="#cit/section_1.20">

128920</a>

1290] Children with mosaic alleles have fewer tumors, and the tumors are more likely to remain unilateral.[<a href="#cit/section_1.21">

129121</a>

1292] In addition, for one specific mutation, the risk of retinoblastoma in a sibling may depend partly on whether the mutation is inherited from the mother or father.[<a href="#cit/section_1.22">

129322</a>

1294] For more information, see <a href="/about-cancer/causes-prevention/genetics/risk-assessment-pdq">

1295Cancer Genetics Risk Assessment and Counseling</a>

1296.</p>

1297</section>

1298<section id="_528">

1299<h3 id="_528_toc">

1300Postdiagnosis Surveillance</h3>

1301<p id="_529" tabindex="-1">

1302Children with a germline <em class="gene-name">

1303RB1</em>

1304 mutation may continue to develop new tumors for a few years after diagnosis and treatment. For this reason, these patients need to be examined frequently. It is common practice for examinations to occur every 2 to 4 months for at least 28 months.[<a href="#cit/section_1.23">

130523</a>

1306] The interval between exams is based on the stability of the disease and age of the child (i.e., less frequent visits as the child ages).</p>

1307<p id="_645" tabindex="-1">

1308A proportion of children who present with unilateral retinoblastoma will eventually develop disease in the opposite eye. Periodic examinations of the unaffected eye are performed until the germline status of the <em class="gene-name">

1309RB1</em>

1310 gene is determined.</p>

1311<p id="_788" tabindex="-1">

1312Because of the poor prognosis for patients with trilateral retinoblastoma, screening with neuroimaging until age 5 years is a common practice in the monitoring of children with the heritable form of the disease. For more information, see the <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_496">

1313Trilateral retinoblastoma</a>

1314 section.</p>

1315</section>

1316<section id="_493">

1317<h3 id="_493_toc">

1318Causes of Retinoblastoma-Related Mortality</h3>

1319<p id="_508" tabindex="-1">

1320While retinoblastoma is a highly curable disease, the challenge is to preserve life and to prevent the loss of an eye, blindness, and other serious effects of treatment that reduce the patient's life span or quality of life. With improvements in the diagnosis and management of retinoblastoma over the past several decades, metastatic retinoblastoma is observed less frequently in the United States and other developed nations. As a result, other causes, such as trilateral retinoblastoma and subsequent neoplasms (SNs), have become significant contributors to retinoblastoma-related mortality in the first and subsequent decades of life. In the United States, before the advent of chemoreduction as a means of treating heritable or bilateral disease and the implementation of neuroimaging screening, trilateral retinoblastoma contributed to more than 50% of retinoblastoma-related mortality for patients in the first decade after their diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.24">

132124</a>

1322] For more information about SNs, see the <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_525">

1323Late Effects From Retinoblastoma Therapy</a>

1324 section.</p>

1325<section id="_496">

1326<h4 id="_496_toc">

1327Trilateral retinoblastoma</h4>

1328<p id="_497" tabindex="-1">

1329Trilateral retinoblastoma is a well-recognized syndrome that occurs in 5% to 15% of patients with heritable retinoblastoma. It is defined by the development of an asynchronous intracranial

1330midline neuroblastic tumor, which typically develops between the ages of 20 and 36 months.[<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

133125</a>

1332] </p>

1333<p id="_846" tabindex="-1">

1334Because of its poor prognosis and the apparent improved survival with early detection and aggressive treatment, screening with routine neuroimaging could potentially detect most cases within 2 years of first diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.25">

133525</a>

1336] Routine baseline brain MRI is recommended at diagnosis because it may detect trilateral retinoblastoma at a subclinical stage. In a small series, the 5-year overall survival rate was 67% for patients with tumors that were detected at baseline, compared with 11% for the group with a delayed diagnosis.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

133726</a>

1338] </p>

1339<p id="_1856" tabindex="-1">

1340Although it is not clear whether early diagnosis can impact survival, screening with MRI has been recommended as often as every 6 months for 5 years for patients suspected of having heritable disease or those with unilateral disease and a positive family history.[<a href="#cit/section_1.27">

134127</a>

1342] Computed tomography scans are generally avoided for routine screening in these children because of the risk related to ionizing radiation exposure.</p>

1343<p id="_792" tabindex="-1">

1344A cystic pineal gland, which is commonly detected by surveillance MRI, needs to be distinguished from a cystic variant of pineoblastoma. In children without retinoblastoma, the incidence of pineal cysts has been reported to be 55.8%.[<a href="#cit/section_1.28">

134528</a>

1346] In a case-control study that included 77 children with retinoblastoma and 77 controls, the incidence of pineal cysts was similar (61% and 69%, respectively), and the size and volume of the pineal gland was not significantly different between the groups.[<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

134729</a>

1348] However, a cystic component has been described in up to 57% of patients with histologically confirmed trilateral retinoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_1.26">

134926</a>

1350] An excessive increase in the size of the pineal gland seems to be the strongest parameter indicating a malignant process.[<a href="#cit/section_1.29">

135129</a>

1352]</p>

1353</section>

1354</section>

1476

1477

1478

1479 <section id="_13">

1480

1481 <h2>

1482Tumor Pathology of Retinoblastoma</h2>

1483

1484 <div id="_section_13" class="pdq-sections">

1485<p id="_14" tabindex="-1">

1486Maturing cone precursor cells appear to be the cell of origin in human retinoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_2.1">

14871</a>

1488,<a href="#cit/section_2.2">

14892</a>

1490] Microscopically, the appearance of retinoblastoma depends on the degree of differentiation. Undifferentiated retinoblastoma is composed of small, round, densely packed cells with hypochromatic nuclei and scant cytoplasm. Several degrees of photoreceptor differentiation have been described and are characterized by distinctive arrangements of tumor cells, as follows:</p>

1491<div class="pdq-content-list">

1492<ul id="_808">

1493<li>

1494<strong>

1495Flexner-Wintersteiner rosettes</strong>

1496 are specific to retinoblastoma. These structures consist of a cluster of low columnar cells arranged around a central lumen that is bounded by an eosinophilic membrane analogous to the external membrane of the normal retina. The lumen contains rosettes that are seen in 70% of tumors.</li>

1497<li>

1498<strong>

1499Homer Wright rosettes</strong>

1500 are composed of irregular circlets of tumor cells arranged around a tangle of fibrils with no lumen or internal-limiting membrane. Homer Wright rosettes are infrequently seen in retinoblastoma and are most often seen in other neuroblastic tumors, such as neuroblastoma and medulloblastoma.

1501</li>

1502</ul>

1503</div>

1504<p id="_662" tabindex="-1">

1505Retinoblastomas

1506are characterized by marked cell proliferation, as evidenced by high mitosis

1507counts, extremely high MIB-1 labeling indices, and strong diffuse nuclear immunoreactivity for <em class="gene-name">

1508CRX</em>

1509, a useful marker to discriminate retinoblastoma from other malignant, small, round cell tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_2.3">

15103</a>

1511,<a href="#cit/section_2.4">

15124</a>

1513]</p>

1514<p id="_735" tabindex="-1">

1515Cavitary retinoblastoma, a rare variant of retinoblastoma, has ophthalmoscopically visible lucent cavities within the tumor. The cavitary spaces appear hollow on ultrasonography and hypofluorescent on angiography. Histopathologically, the cavitary spaces have been shown to represent areas of photoreceptor differentiation.[<a href="#cit/section_2.5">

15165</a>

1517]</p>

1518<p id="_1846" tabindex="-1">

1519Cavitary retinoblastoma has been associated with minimal visible response to intravenous and intra-arterial chemotherapy, which is thought to be a sign of tumor differentiation.[<a href="#cit/section_2.6">

15206</a>

1521,<a href="#cit/section_2.7">

15227</a>

1523] Despite the blunted clinical response, patients with cavitary retinoblastoma have favorable long-term outcomes, with good tumor response and globe salvage that is similar to that in patients with noncavitary retinoblastoma.</p>

1524<p id="_809" tabindex="-1">

1525A pathologist experienced in ocular pathology and retinoblastoma should examine the enucleated specimen, particularly to determine risk features of extraocular dissemination. For more information, see the <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_740">

1526Treatment of Intraocular Retinoblastoma</a>

1527 section.</p>

1566 <section id="_15">

1567

1568 <h2>

1569Staging and Grouping Systems for Retinoblastoma</h2>

1570

1571 <div id="_section_15" class="pdq-sections">

1572<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

1573<h6>

1574In This Section</h6>

1575<ul>

1576<li>

1577<a href="#_17_toc">

1578Intraocular Retinoblastoma</a>

1579</li>

1580<li>

1581<a href="#_20_toc">

1582Extraocular Retinoblastoma</a>

1583</li>

1584<li>

1585<a href="#_613_toc">

1586Staging Systems<ul>

1587<li>

1588<a href="#_607_toc">

1589American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC) staging system</a>

1590</li>

1591<li>

1592<a href="#_611_toc">

1593International Retinoblastoma Staging System (IRSS)</a>

1594</li>

1595</ul>

1596</a>

1597</li>

1598<li>

1599<a href="#_614_toc">

1600Grouping Systems<ul>

1601<li>

1602<a href="#_509_toc">

1603International Classification of Retinoblastoma</a>

1604</li>

1605<li>

1606<a href="#_23_toc">

1607Reese-Ellsworth Classification for Intraocular Tumors</a>

1608</li>

1609</ul>

1610</a>

1611</li>

1612</ul>

1613</nav>

1614<p id="_640" tabindex="-1">

1615The staging of patients with retinoblastoma requires close coordination of radiologists, pediatric oncologists, and ophthalmologists. Several staging and grouping systems have been proposed for retinoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_3.1">

16161</a>

1617] Overall assessment of retinoblastoma extension is documented by staging systems. The extent of intraocular disease, which is relevant for ocular salvage, is documented by grouping systems. For treatment purposes, retinoblastoma is categorized

1618into intraocular and extraocular disease.

1619</p>

1620<section id="_17">

1621<h3 id="_17_toc">

1622Intraocular Retinoblastoma</h3>

1623<p id="_19" tabindex="-1">

1624Intraocular retinoblastoma is localized to the eye. It may be confined to the

1625retina or may extend to involve other structures such as the choroid, ciliary body, anterior chamber, and optic nerve head. Intraocular retinoblastoma, however, does not extend beyond

1626the eye into the tissues around the eye or to other parts of the body.

1627</p>

1628</section>

1629<section id="_20">

1630<h3 id="_20_toc">

1631Extraocular Retinoblastoma</h3>

1632<p id="_22" tabindex="-1">

1633Extraocular retinoblastoma extends beyond the eye. It may be confined to

1634the tissues around the eye (orbital retinoblastoma), it may have spread to the central

1635nervous system, or it may have spread systemically to the bone marrow or lymph nodes (metastatic retinoblastoma).

1636</p>

1637</section>

1638<section id="_613">

1639<h3 id="_613_toc">

1640Staging Systems</h3>

1641<section id="_607">

1642<h4 id="_607_toc">

1643American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC) staging system</h4>

1644<p id="_608" tabindex="-1">

1645Several staging systems have been proposed over the years. The newest standard for state-mandated cancer reporting to the North American Association of Cancer Registries requires AJCC staging, according to the 8th edition of the staging manual.[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

16462</a>

1647] </p>

1648<p id="_2840" tabindex="-1">

1649For information about the <strong>

1650clinical</strong>

1651 classification definitions of primary tumor (T), regional lymph node (N), distant metastasis (M), histological grade, and prognostic stage groups, see <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_2836">

1652Table 3</a>

1653, <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_2837">

1654Table 5</a>

1655, <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1895">

1656Table 7</a>

1657, <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1896">

1658Table 8</a>

1659, and <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_2838">

1660Table 9</a>

1661. </p>

1662<p id="_2841" tabindex="-1">

1663For information about the <strong>

1664pathological</strong>

1665 classification definitions of T, N, M, histological grade, and prognostic stage groups, see <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1893">

1666Table 4</a>

1667, <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1894">

1668Table 6</a>

1669, <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1895">

1670Table 7</a>

1671, <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1896">

1672Table 8</a>

1673, and <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1897">

1674Table 10</a>

1675. </p>

1676<p id="_2839" tabindex="-1">

1677This staging system affects cases diagnosed in 2018 and thereafter. Retinoblastoma staging is the first to acknowledge the role of genetic predisposition by incorporating an <em>

1678H</em>

1679 category. H1 refers to patients with bilateral or trilateral retinoblastoma, a family history of retinoblastoma, or the presence of an <em class="gene-name">

1680RB1</em>

1681 mutation (see <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1892">

1682Table 2</a>

1683).[<a href="#cit/section_3.2">

16842</a>

1685]</p>

2448<section id="_611">

2449<h4 id="_611_toc">

2450International Retinoblastoma Staging System (IRSS)</h4>

2451<p id="_612" tabindex="-1">

2452The more simplified IRSS has been proposed by an international consortium of ophthalmologists and pediatric oncologists.[<a href="#cit/section_3.3">

24533</a>

2454] The IRSS is more widely used in the clinical setting than the AJCC staging system (see <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_631">

2455Table 11</a>

2456). A retrospective German study found that the IRSS predicted survival in 633 children with heritable retinoblastoma, 582 of whom presented with IRSS stage 0 or I disease.[<a href="#cit/section_3.4">

24574</a>

2458]</p>

2459<table id="_631" class="table-default expandable-container">

2549</section>

2550<section id="_614">

2551<h3 id="_614_toc">

2552Grouping Systems</h3>

2553<p id="_615" tabindex="-1">

2554The following grouping systems are relevant for assessment of intraocular disease extension and are helpful predictors of ocular salvage:</p>

2555<div class="pdq-content-list">

2556<ul id="_1862">

2557<li>

2558<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_509">

2559International Classification of Retinoblastoma</a>

2560.</li>

2561<li>

2562<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1897">

2563American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC), 8th edition</a>

2564.</li>

2565<li>

2566<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_23">

2567Reese-Ellsworth classification for intraocular tumors (historical)</a>

2568.</li>

2569</ul>

2570</div>

2571<section id="_509">

2572<h4 id="_509_toc">

2573International Classification of Retinoblastoma</h4>

2574<p id="_510" tabindex="-1">

2575The International Classification of Retinoblastoma grouping system was developed with the goal of providing a simpler, more user-friendly classification that is more applicable to current therapies. This newer system is based on the extent of tumor seeding within the vitreous cavity and subretinal space, rather than on tumor size and location (see <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1871">

2576Table 12</a>

2577). The use of this system seems to better predict treatment success.[<a href="#cit/section_3.5">

25785</a>

2579-<a href="#cit/section_3.7">

25807</a>

2581] This system may also help predict high-risk histopathology. In a study of more than 500 patients with retinoblastoma, histopathological evidence of high-risk disease was noted in 17% of Group D eyes and 24% of Group E eyes. This can be helpful in counseling parents regarding the potential need for postoperative systemic therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_3.8">

25828</a>

2583]</p>

2722<section id="_23">

2723<h4 id="_23_toc">

2724Reese-Ellsworth Classification for Intraocular Tumors</h4>

2725<p id="_24" tabindex="-1">

2726 Reese and Ellsworth developed a classification system for

2727intraocular retinoblastoma that has been shown to have prognostic significance

2728for maintenance of sight and control of local disease at a time when surgery and external-beam radiation therapy were the primary treatment options. However, developments in the conservative management of intraocular retinoblastoma have made the <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=782667&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000782667&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet'); return(false);">

2729Reese-Ellsworth grouping system</a>

2730 less predictive for eye salvage and less helpful in guiding treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_3.6">

27316</a>

2732] This grouping system is seldom used and serves largely as a historical reference.</p>

2733</section>

2773 <section id="_53">

2774

2775 <h2>

2776Treatment Option Overview for Retinoblastoma</h2>

2777

2778 <div id="_section_53" class="pdq-sections">

2779<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

2780<h6>

2781In This Section</h6>

2782<ul>

2783<li>

2784<a href="#_811_toc">

2785Enucleation</a>

2786</li>

2787<li>

2788<a href="#_813_toc">

2789Local Treatment (Cryotherapy, Laser Therapy, and Brachytherapy)</a>

2790</li>

2791<li>

2792<a href="#_816_toc">

2793Systemic Chemotherapy</a>

2794</li>

2795<li>

2796<a href="#_821_toc">

2797Ophthalmic Artery Infusion of Chemotherapy (Intra-arterial Chemotherapy)</a>

2798</li>

2799<li>

2800<a href="#_824_toc">

2801Intravitreal Chemotherapy</a>

2802</li>

2803<li>

2804<a href="#_2817_toc">

2805Intracameral Chemotherapy</a>

2806</li>

2807<li>

2808<a href="#_830_toc">

2809Radiation Therapy</a>

2810</li>

2811</ul>

2812</nav>

2813<p id="_54" tabindex="-1">

2814Treatment planning by a multidisciplinary team of cancer specialists&#8212;including a pediatric oncologist, ophthalmologist, and radiation oncologist&#8212;with

2815experience treating ocular tumors of childhood is required to optimize treatment outcomes.[<a href="#cit/section_4.1">

28161</a>

2817] Evaluation at specialized centers is highly recommended before the initiation of treatment to improve the likelihood of ocular salvage and vision preservation.</p>

2818<p id="_512" tabindex="-1">

2819The goals of therapy include the following: </p>

2820<div class="pdq-content-list">

2821<ul id="_663">

2822<li>

2823Eradicate the disease to save the patient's life.</li>

2824<li>

2825Preserve as much vision as possible.</li>

2826<li>

2827Decrease risk of late sequelae from treatment, particularly subsequent neoplasms (SNs).</li>

2828</ul>

2829</div>

2830<p id="_1863" tabindex="-1">

2831 Many treatments considered to be standard of care have not been studied in a randomized fashion.</p>

2832<p id="_736" tabindex="-1">

2833Treatment of retinoblastoma depends on the intraocular and extraocular disease burden, disease laterality, germline <em class="gene-name">

2834RB1</em>

2835 gene status, and the potential for preserving vision. For patients presenting with intraocular disease, particularly those with bilateral eye involvement, a conservative approach consisting of tumor reduction with intravenous or ophthalmic artery chemotherapy, coupled with aggressive local therapy, may result in high ocular salvage rates.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

28362</a>

2837] Radiation therapy, one of the most effective treatments in retinoblastoma, is usually reserved for cases of intraocular or extraocular disease progression.</p>

2838<p id="_737" tabindex="-1">

2839A risk-adapted, judicious combination of the following therapeutic options should be considered:</p>

2840<div class="pdq-content-list">

2841<ul id="_810">

2842<li>

2843<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_811">

2844Enucleation</a>

2845.</li>

2846<li>

2847<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_813">

2848Local treatment (cryotherapy, laser therapy, and brachytherapy)</a>

2849.</li>

2850<li>

2851<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_816">

2852Systemic chemotherapy</a>

2853.</li>

2854<li>

2855<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_821">

2856Ophthalmic artery infusion of chemotherapy (intra-arterial chemotherapy)</a>

2857.</li>

2858<li>

2859<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_824">

2860Intravitreal chemotherapy</a>

2861.</li>

2862<li>

2863<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_2817">

2864Intracameral chemotherapy</a>

2865.</li>

2866<li>

2867<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_830">

2868Radiation therapy (external-beam radiation therapy [EBRT], brachytherapy)</a>

2869.</li>

2870</ul>

2871</div>

2873The treatment options for intraocular, extraocular, and recurrent retinoblastoma are described in <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_749">

2874Table 13</a>

2875.</p>

3077<section id="_811">

3078<h3 id="_811_toc">

3079Enucleation</h3>

3080<p id="_812" tabindex="-1">

3081Upfront removal of the eye is indicated for large tumors filling the vitreous for which there is little or no likelihood of restoring vision, in cases of extension to the anterior chamber, or in the presence of neovascular glaucoma. Patients must be monitored closely for orbital recurrence of disease, particularly in the first 2 years after enucleation.[<a href="#cit/section_4.3">

30823</a>

3083][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3084Level of evidence C1</a>

3085] Enucleation is also used as a salvage treatment in cases of disease progression or recurrence in patients receiving eye-salvage management. The pathology specimen must be carefully examined to identify patients who are at high risk of extraocular dissemination and who may require adjuvant chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

30864</a>

3087][Level of evidence <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3088C1</a>

3089 and <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3090C2</a>

3091]</p>

3092<p id="_1847" tabindex="-1">

3093Enucleation in patients younger than 3 years does not allow for the proper orbital growth during subsequent development, causing asymmetry of the final orbital size.[<a href="#cit/section_4.5">

30945</a>

3095]</p>

3096</section>

3097<section id="_813">

3098<h3 id="_813_toc">

3099Local Treatment (Cryotherapy, Laser Therapy, and Brachytherapy)</h3>

3100<p id="_814" tabindex="-1">

3101For patients undergoing eye-salvage treatments, aggressive local therapy is always required. Local treatment is administered by the ophthalmologist directly to the tumor.</p>

3102<div class="pdq-content-list">

3103<ul id="_815">

3104<li>

3105<strong>

3106Cryotherapy.</strong>

3107 Cryotherapy is based on the application of a cryoprobe to the sclera in the immediate vicinity of the retinal tumor. Cryotherapy is used as primary therapy or with chemotherapy for tumors smaller than 4 disc diameters (DD) in the

3108anterior portion of the retina.

3109</li>

3110<li>

3111<strong>

3112Laser therapy.</strong>

3113 Laser therapy may be used as primary therapy for small tumors or in combination with chemotherapy for larger tumors. Traditional photocoagulation (argon laser), in which the laser was applied around the tumor to target the tumor vasculature, has given way to thermotherapy (diode laser). Thermotherapy is delivered directly to the tumor surface via infrared wavelengths of light.[<a href="#cit/section_4.6">

31146</a>

3115,<a href="#cit/section_4.7">

31167</a>

3117]</li>

3118<li>

3119<strong>

3120Brachytherapy (plaque radiation therapy).</strong>

3121 For larger tumors that are not amenable to cryotherapy or laser therapy, brachytherapy can provide an effective means for local control. For more information, see the <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_830">

3122Radiation Therapy</a>

3123 section.</li>

3124</ul>

3125</div>

3126</section>

3127<section id="_816">

3128<h3 id="_816_toc">

3129Systemic Chemotherapy</h3>

3130<p id="_817" tabindex="-1">

3131Systemic chemotherapy plays a role in the following:</p>

3132<div class="pdq-content-list">

3133<ul id="_818">

3134<li>

3135<strong>

3136Adjuvant setting for patients with high-risk pathology.</strong>

3137 Different regimens have been used in the management of patients with high-risk pathology in the enucleated specimen. Most regimens include a three-drug combination of vincristine, etoposide, and carboplatin, either alone or alternating with cyclophosphamide and an anthracycline.[<a href="#cit/section_4.8">

31388</a>

3139-<a href="#cit/section_4.11">

314011</a>

3141]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.4">

31424</a>

3143][Level of evidence <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3144C1</a>

3145 and <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3146C2</a>

3147]</li>

3148<li>

3149<strong>

3150Treatment of patients with extraocular and metastatic disease.</strong>

3151 Patients with extraocular disease benefit from more intensive therapy. While a standard treatment has not been determined, responses to cisplatin-based regimens, with consolidation using high-dose chemotherapy and autologous hematopoietic stem cell rescue for patients with extraorbital disease, have been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_4.12">

315212</a>

3153-<a href="#cit/section_4.15">

315415</a>

3155]</li>

3156<li>

3157<strong>

3158Chemoreductive treatment in conjunction with aggressive local treatment for patients undergoing ocular salvage treatments.</strong>

3159 During the past two decades, the standard of care has been systemic chemotherapy to reduce tumor

3160volume (chemoreduction) to facilitate the use of local treatments and to avoid the long-term effects of radiation

3161therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

316216</a>

3163] The success rate for eye salvage varies from center to center, but overall good ocular outcomes are consistently obtained for discrete tumors without vitreous seeding. In a large cohort analysis of 994 eyes in 554 patients who were treated with intravenous chemotherapy and had long-term outcome data, investigators found that tumor control was strongly dependent on the International Classification of Retinoblastoma group designation per eye. Frontline intravenous chemotherapy consisting of six cycles of vincristine, etoposide, and carboplatin plus additional intra-arterial chemotherapy and/or plaque radiation therapy led to tumor control for groups A (96%), B (91%), C (91%), D (71%), and E (32%) by year 2. With the aforementioned treatment, enucleation or external-beam radiation therapy could be avoided, and the tumor-controlling effect lasted up to 20 years.[<a href="#cit/section_4.17">

316417</a>

3165][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3166Level of evidence C1</a>

3167]<p id="_1864" tabindex="-1">

3168Eye grouping, as defined by the <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_509">

3169International Classification of Retinoblastoma</a>

3170, is the best predictor of ocular salvage using this approach, with salvage rates ranging from 60% to 100%.[<a href="#cit/section_4.16">

317116</a>

3172]</p>

3173<p id="_1865" tabindex="-1">

3174 Prolonged chemotherapy instead of enucleation, in the context of persistent intraocular disease activity, should be used cautiously because this approach has been associated with an increased risk of metastatic disease.[<a href="#cit/section_4.18">

317518</a>

3176]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.19">

317719</a>

3178][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3179Level of evidence C2</a>

3180] </p>

3181</li>

3182</ul>

3183</div>

3184</section>

3185<section id="_821">

3186<h3 id="_821_toc">

3187Ophthalmic Artery Infusion of Chemotherapy (Intra-arterial Chemotherapy)</h3>

3188<p id="_822" tabindex="-1">

3189Direct delivery of chemotherapy into the eye via cannulation of the ophthalmic artery is a feasible and effective method for ocular salvage. Responses to chemotherapy using this approach can be further consolidated with local control measures, as described above.</p>

3190<p id="_793" tabindex="-1">

3191Melphalan is the most common and most effective agent used for intra-arterial chemotherapy. It is often combined with topotecan or carboplatin when responses are suboptimal or there is very advanced intraocular disease.[<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

319220</a>

3193,<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

319421</a>

3195]</p>

3196<p id="_794" tabindex="-1">

3197For patients with treatment-naive eyes, the 2-year radiation-free ocular survival rate is 86% to 90%.[<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

319820</a>

3199,<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

320021</a>

3201] Outcome after intra-arterial chemotherapy correlates with the extent of intraocular burden, as follows: </p>

3202<div class="pdq-content-list">

3203<ul id="_823">

3204<li>

3205Patients with early intraocular disease (Group B and C eyes) have an excellent outcome, with ocular salvage rates exceeding 85%. These patients may be treated with single-agent therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

320621</a>

3207]</li>

3208<li>

3209Patients with Group D eyes have a worse outcome.[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

321021</a>

3211] However, ocular salvage rates higher than 80% have been reported in specialized centers.[<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

321220</a>

3213,<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

321422</a>

3215] For patients with very advanced intraocular disease, an alternative treatment is the use of systemic chemotherapy followed by consolidation with intra-arterial melphalan.[<a href="#cit/section_4.23">

321623</a>

3217]</li>

3218<li>

3219Ocular salvage rates when intra-arterial chemotherapy administration is used as salvage treatment for patients with recurrent or progressive disease are consistently lower, with globe survival rates of 50% to 75%.[<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

322020</a>

3221-<a href="#cit/section_4.22">

322222</a>

3223] Best results are reported using a more intensive three-drug regimen with melphalan, topotecan, and carboplatin.[<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

322424</a>

3225] </li>

3226</ul>

3227</div>

3228<p id="_795" tabindex="-1">

3229Patients with bilateral disease can undergo tandem intra-arterial chemotherapy administration.[<a href="#cit/section_4.25">

323025</a>

3231] In those circumstances, patients are at higher risk of systemic toxicity caused by melphalan exposure,[<a href="#cit/section_4.26">

323226</a>

3233] and single-agent carboplatin may be used to treat the less-advanced eye during the tandem procedure.[<a href="#cit/section_4.27">

323427</a>

3235] For neonates and very young infants in whom the cannulization of the ophthalmic artery is not feasible, <em>

3236bridge</em>

3237 treatment with single-agent systemic carboplatin until the infant is aged 3 months or weighs 6 kg, followed by consolidation with intra-arterial chemotherapy, has been shown to be very effective, with a 1-year radiation-free ocular survival rate of 95%.[<a href="#cit/section_4.28">

323828</a>

3239] In a study of 39 infants younger than 3 months with advanced intraocular retinoblastoma (group D and E eyes), patients received intra-arterial chemotherapy as primary treatment (29 eyes) or secondary treatment (13 eyes previously treated with IV chemotherapy) using a microcatheterization procedure. The middle meningeal artery was used when the ophthalmic artery could not be catheterized. The globe salvage rate was 96% for the 23 group D eyes and 33% for the 6 group E eyes, but the 2-year overall ocular survival rate was 81% because of an increased incidence of phthisis bulbi. The incidence of complete vision loss was high. Follow-up was limited, ranging from 6 months to 6 years; therefore, later effects are unknown.[<a href="#cit/section_4.29">

324029</a>

3241]</p>

3242<p id="_796" tabindex="-1">

3243The addition of intravitreal chemotherapy to intra-arterial chemotherapy appears to markedly improve the overall effectiveness in eyes with vitreous seeds, especially those with vitreous seed clouds.[<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

324420</a>

3245,<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

324630</a>

3247,<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

324831</a>

3249] For more information, see the <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_824">

3250Intravitreal Chemotherapy</a>

3251 section. In patients presenting with total retinal detachment, ophthalmic artery chemosurgery has been shown to promote retinal reattachment.[<a href="#cit/section_4.32">

325232</a>

3253]</p>

3254<p id="_797" tabindex="-1">

3255Complications related to intra-arterial chemotherapy include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

325621</a>

3257,<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

325833</a>

3259]</p>

3260<div class="pdq-content-list">

3261<ul id="_798">

3262<li>

3263Retinal detachment (up to 19.3%).[<a href="#cit/section_4.34">

326434</a>

3265]</li>

3266<li>

3267Vitreous hemorrhage (up to 18.1%).</li>

3268<li>

3269Ptosis (13.6%).</li>

3270<li>

3271Dysmotility (6.5%).</li>

3272<li>

3273Vascular and ischemic effects, including ophthalmic artery occlusion (up to 9%).</li>

3274<li>

3275Optic atrophy (3.4%).</li>

3276<li>

3277Phthisis (2.7%).</li>

3278</ul>

3279</div>

3280<p id="_799" tabindex="-1">

3281Major vascular complications related to the procedure are very rare. Strokes or significant acute neurological events have not been reported by the most experienced groups.[<a href="#cit/section_4.20">

328220</a>

3283,<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

328421</a>

3285,<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

328635</a>

3287] However, stenosis of the ophthalmic artery and occlusion of the retinal artery have been documented.[<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

328833</a>

3289,<a href="#cit/section_4.35">

329035</a>

3291] The risk of thrombosis is significantly increased in children with thrombophilia.[<a href="#cit/section_4.36">

329236</a>

3293] In a large series of 196 patients who were treated with 682 infusions of intra-arterial chemotherapy, ophthalmic vascular events were reported in 17% of the treated eyes.[<a href="#cit/section_4.33">

329433</a>

3295]</p>

3296<p id="_800" tabindex="-1">

3297The impact of the intraocular vascular changes on vision has not been fully assessed because of the young age of the first cohorts of patients treated. Most patients do not have substantial electroretinographic changes,[<a href="#cit/section_4.37">

329837</a>

3299] and preservation of central vision has been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_4.38">

330038</a>

3301] A proportion of patients with abnormal electroretinograms (ERGs) with or without retinal detachment may have improved ERGs in the years after intra-arterial chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.39">

330239</a>

3303] However, in patients with heavily pretreated eyes, intensive intra-arterial chemotherapy may result in worsening of retinal function.[<a href="#cit/section_4.24">

330424</a>

3305]</p>

3306<p id="_801" tabindex="-1">

3307Another risk associated with intra-arterial chemotherapy is the exposure to ionizing radiation during fluoroscopy. Mean total radiation doses of 42.3 mGy have been reported in very experienced centers.[<a href="#cit/section_4.40">

330840</a>

3309] After multiple procedures, cumulative doses can reach 0.1 to 0.2 Gy, which can be cataractogenic and potentially carcinogenic in this susceptible population.[<a href="#cit/section_4.41">

331041</a>

3311] There is no increase in the incidence of second malignancies.[<a href="#cit/section_4.42">

331242</a>

3313,<a href="#cit/section_4.43">

331443</a>

3315] However, longer follow-up will be required to fully ascertain the risks associated with the procedure.</p>

3316<p id="_802" tabindex="-1">

3317The risk of metastatic progression with direct ocular delivery of chemotherapy appears to be very low.[<a href="#cit/section_4.2">

33182</a>

3319] However, up to 20 cases of patients treated with intra-arterial chemotherapy who subsequently developed metastases have been reported.[<a href="#cit/section_4.21">

332021</a>

3321]</p>

3322</section>

3323<section id="_824">

3324<h3 id="_824_toc">

3325Intravitreal Chemotherapy</h3>

3326<p id="_825" tabindex="-1">

3327 Studies suggest that direct intravitreal injection of melphalan or topotecan may be effective in controlling active vitreous seeds.[<a href="#cit/section_4.44">

332844</a>

3329,<a href="#cit/section_4.45">

333045</a>

3331]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.46">

333246</a>

3333,<a href="#cit/section_4.47">

333447</a>

3335][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3336Level of evidence C2</a>

3337] A retrospective study of 264 eyes (250 children) treated with intravitreal melphalan for vitreous seeds over a two-decade period reported a complete remission rate of 68%. There was a low incidence of extraocular spread as a result of the injection that occurred in children with high-risk features.[<a href="#cit/section_4.48">

333848</a>

3339][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3340Level of evidence C2</a>

3341]</p>

3342<p id="_1866" tabindex="-1">

3343 Because of initial concerns about the potential for tumor dissemination, the use of intravitreal chemotherapy was limited. However, additional reports have estimated that the proportion of patients with extraocular tumor spread, as the result of intravitreal injection, is negligible.[<a href="#cit/section_4.49">

334449</a>

3345,<a href="#cit/section_4.50">

334650</a>

3347] While this procedure is safe and well tolerated, recent studies have shown a direct correlation between the number of injections and a decrease in retinal function, as measured by ERG.[<a href="#cit/section_4.50">

334850</a>

3349]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.51">

335051</a>

3351][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3352Level of evidence C3</a>

3353] </p>

3354<p id="_1840" tabindex="-1">

3355Preliminary data seem to support that intra-arterial chemotherapy plus intravitreal chemotherapy (as needed for vitreous seeding) may improve globe salvage in eyes with advanced retinoblastoma when compared with children who were treated in earlier years with intra-arterial chemotherapy alone.[<a href="#cit/section_4.50">

335650</a>

3357]; [<a href="#cit/section_4.30">

335830</a>

3359][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3360Level of evidence C2</a>

3361] Compared with the children treated in the earlier era, children treated in the later era received a combination of intra-arterial and intra-vitreal chemotherapy, which demonstrated shorter time to regression, fewer recurrences, fewer enucleations, and no increased toxicity, including no difference in loss of retinal function as measured by ERG.[<a href="#cit/section_4.31">

336231</a>

3363][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3364Level of evidence C3</a>

3365]</p>

3366<p id="_1841" tabindex="-1">

3367As experience with the use of intra-vitreal chemotherapy expands, studies have demonstrated its efficacy in controlling subretinal seeds and recurrent retinal tumors, suggesting a potential role beyond the control of vitreous seeds as an adjunctive therapy in the globe-sparing treatment of retinoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_4.52">

336852</a>

3369]</p>

3370</section>

3371<section id="_2817">

3372<h3 id="_2817_toc">

3373Intracameral Chemotherapy</h3>

3374<p id="_2818" tabindex="-1">

3375An alternative treatment for anterior chamber seeding is injection of melphalan into the aqueous humor. A retrospective, single-institution study reported ocular salvage in 6 of 11 eyes treated with intracameral melphalan (median, four injections), with a mean follow-up of 17 months.[<a href="#cit/section_4.53">

337653</a>

3377]</p>

3378</section>

3379<section id="_830">

3380<h3 id="_830_toc">

3381Radiation Therapy</h3>

3382<div class="pdq-content-list">

3383<ul id="_831">

3384<li>

3385<strong>

3386EBRT.</strong>

3387 Retinoblastoma is a very radiosensitive malignancy. EBRT doses ranging from 35 Gy to 46 Gy usually result in long-term remissions.

3388Because of the need to sedate young children and the intricacies of field

3389planning, special expertise in pediatric radiation therapy is important.

3390 Radiation therapy is used in cases of progression after conservative approaches, in patients with extraocular extension, and as part of the management of patients with metastatic disease.<p id="_760" tabindex="-1">

3391Newer methods of delivering EBRT are being applied to reduce adverse long-term effects. This includes <a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=335073&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000335073&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet'); return(false);">

3392intensity-modulated radiation therapy</a>

3393 and proton-beam radiation therapy (<a class="definition" type="GlossaryTermRefs" href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=534234&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000534234&amp;version=healthprofessional&amp;language=English&amp;dictionary=NotSet'); return(false);">

3394charged-particle radiation therapy</a>

3395).[<a href="#cit/section_4.54">

339654</a>

3397-<a href="#cit/section_4.57">

339857</a>

3399] Preliminary data suggest that proton radiation therapy is associated with a lower risk of radiation-induced malignancy in survivors of heritable retinoblastoma. In a nonrandomized study that compared two contemporary cohorts of patients with heritable retinoblastoma who were treated with either photon or proton radiation therapy, the 10-year cumulative incidence of radiation-induced SNs was significantly different between the two groups (0% for proton radiation vs. 14% for photon radiation, <em>

3400P</em>

3401 = .015).[<a href="#cit/section_4.58">

340258</a>

3403]</p>

3404<p id="_666" tabindex="-1">

3405EBRT in infants causes growth failure of the orbital bones and results in cosmetic deformity. EBRT also increases the risk of SNs in children with heritable retinoblastoma.</p>

3406</li>

3407<li>

3408<strong>

3409Brachytherapy (plaque radiation therapy).</strong>

3410 Indications for plaque radiation therapy include solitary tumors with a diameter ranging between 6 mm and 15 mm, tumor thickness of 10 mm or less, and tumor location of more than 3 mm from the optic disc or fovea. The most commonly used radioisotope is iodine I 125, although others such as iridium Ir 192 and ruthenium Ru 106 are also effective. In combination with the appropriate use of chemotherapy and other forms of focal consolidation, brachytherapy can be very effective in the treatment of localized retinal tumors that are not amenable to other means of local therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_4.59">

341159</a>

3412-<a href="#cit/section_4.61">

341361</a>

3414]</li>

3415</ul>

3416</div>

3417</section>

3672 <section id="_841">

3673

3674 <h2>

3675Special Considerations for the Treatment of Children With Cancer</h2>

3676

3677 <div id="_section_841" class="pdq-sections">

3678<p id="_2810" tabindex="-1">

3679Cancer in children and adolescents is rare, although the overall incidence has been slowly increasing since 1975.[<a href="#cit/section_5.1">

36801</a>

3681] Referral to medical centers with multidisciplinary teams of cancer specialists experienced in treating cancers that occur in childhood and adolescence should be considered for children and adolescents with cancer. This multidisciplinary team approach incorporates the skills

3682of the following health care professionals and others to ensure that children receive treatment, supportive care, and rehabilitation

3683that will achieve optimal survival and quality of life:</p>

3684<div class="pdq-content-list">

3685<ul id="_843">

3686<li>

3687An ophthalmologist with extensive experience in the treatment of children with retinoblastoma.</li>

3688<li>

3689Primary care physicians.</li>

3690<li>

3691Pediatric surgical subspecialists.</li>

3692<li>

3693Radiation oncologists.</li>

3694<li>

3695Pediatric medical oncologists/hematologists.</li>

3696<li>

3697Rehabilitation specialists.</li>

3698<li>

3699Pediatric nurse specialists.</li>

3700<li>

3701Social workers.</li>

3702</ul>

3703</div>

3704<p id="_1867" tabindex="-1">

3705 For specific information about supportive care for children and adolescents with cancer, see the summaries on <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/information-summaries/supportive-care">

3706Supportive and Palliative Care</a>

3707.</p>

3708<p id="_2811" tabindex="-1">

3709The American Academy of Pediatrics has outlined guidelines for

3710pediatric cancer centers and their role in the treatment of pediatric patients

3711with cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_5.2">

37122</a>

3713] At

3714these pediatric cancer centers, clinical trials are available for most types of cancer that occur in children and adolescents, and the opportunity

3715to participate is offered to most patients and their families. Clinical

3716trials for children and adolescents diagnosed with cancer are generally

3717designed to compare potentially better therapy with current standard therapy. Most of the progress made in identifying curative

3718therapy for childhood cancers has been achieved through clinical trials.

3719Information about ongoing clinical trials is available from the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials">

3720NCI website</a>

3721.</p>

3722<p id="_845" tabindex="-1">

3723Dramatic improvements in survival have been achieved for children and adolescents with cancer.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

37243</a>

3725-<a href="#cit/section_5.5">

37265</a>

3727] Between 1975 and 2020, childhood cancer mortality decreased by more than 50%.[<a href="#cit/section_5.3">

37283</a>

3729,<a href="#cit/section_5.6">

37306</a>

3731,<a href="#cit/section_5.7">

37327</a>

3733] Childhood and adolescent cancer survivors require close monitoring because side effects of cancer therapy may persist or develop months or years after treatment. For specific information about the incidence, type, and monitoring of late effects in childhood and adolescent cancer survivors, see <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq">

3734Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer</a>

3735.</p>
