3818<section id="_672">

3819<h3 id="_672_toc">

3820Treatment of Unilateral Intraocular Retinoblastoma</h3>

3821<p id="_673" tabindex="-1">

3822Treatment options for unilateral intraocular retinoblastoma include the following:</p>

3823<div class="pdq-content-list">

3824<ol id="_674">

3825<li>

3826<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_725">

3827Enucleation for large intraocular tumors, with or without adjuvant chemotherapy based on pathology risk, when the eye cannot be saved</a>

3828.</li>

3829<li>

3830<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_726">

3831Conservative ocular salvage approaches when the eye and vision can be saved</a>

3832:<div class="pdq-content-list">

3833<ul id="_675">

3834<li>

3835Chemoreduction with either systemic or ophthalmic artery infusion chemotherapy with or without intravitreal chemotherapy.</li>

3836<li>

3837Local treatments, including cryotherapy, thermotherapy, and plaque radiation therapy.</li>

3838</ul>

3839</div>

3840</li>

3841</ol>

3842</div>

3843<section id="_725">

3844<h4 id="_725_toc">

3845Enucleation with or without adjuvant chemotherapy</h4>

3846<p id="_676" tabindex="-1">

3847Because unilateral disease is usually massive and often there is no

3848expectation that useful vision can be preserved, up-front surgery (enucleation) is

3849commonly performed. Careful examination of the enucleated specimen by an experienced pathologist is necessary to determine whether high-risk features for metastatic disease are present. These <strong>

3850high-risk features</strong>

3851 include the following:[<a href="#cit/section_6.1">

38521</a>

3853-<a href="#cit/section_6.5">

38545</a>

3855]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

38566</a>

3857][Level of evidence <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3858C1</a>

3859 and <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3860C2</a>

3861]</p>

3862<div class="pdq-content-list">

3863<ul id="_677">

3864<li>

3865Anterior chamber seeding.</li>

3866<li>

3867Massive choroidal involvement.</li>

3868<li>

3869Tumor beyond the lamina cribrosa.</li>

3870<li>

3871Scleral and extrascleral extension.</li>

3872</ul>

3873</div>

3874<p id="_678" tabindex="-1">

3875Pre-enucleation magnetic resonance imaging has low sensitivity and specificity for the detection of high-risk pathology.[<a href="#cit/section_6.7">

38767</a>

3877]</p>

3878<p id="_1816" tabindex="-1">

3879High-risk pathology has been associated with the presence of minimal dissemination in bone marrow and cerebrospinal fluid using quantitative polymerase chain reaction for detection of <em class="gene-name">

3880CRX</em>

3881 or GD2 synthase. In a group of 96 children with nonmetastatic retinoblastoma and high-risk pathology, the 3-year disease-free survival rate was 78% for patients with detectable minimal dissemination, compared with 98% for those without detectable disease (<em>

3882P</em>

3883 = .004).[<a href="#cit/section_6.8">

38848</a>

3885]</p>

3886<p id="_742" tabindex="-1">

3887Systemic adjuvant therapy with vincristine, doxorubicin, and cyclophosphamide or with vincristine, carboplatin, and etoposide has been used to prevent the development of metastatic disease in patients with certain high-risk features assessed by pathological review after enucleation.[<a href="#cit/section_6.3">

38883</a>

3889]</p>

3890<p id="_1874" tabindex="-1">

3891The Children&#8217;s Oncology Group <a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00335738">

3892ARET0332 (NCT00335738)</a>

3893 trial prospectively studied the role of adjuvant chemotherapy in 321 eligible children with newly diagnosed enucleated unilateral retinoblastoma. Central histopathological review was performed for all patients' pathology slides. Defined indications for adjuvant chemotherapy included massive choroid replacement defined as posterior uveal invasion grades IIC and IID, any posterior uveal involvement less than 3 mm with concomitant optic nerve involvement, and optic nerve involvement posterior to the lamina cribrosa. Treatment consisted of six cycles of carboplatin, etoposide, and vincristine administered every 4 weeks.[<a href="#cit/section_6.6">

38946</a>

3895][Level of evidence <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3896C1</a>

3897 and <a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810037&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3898C2</a>

3899]</p>

3900<div class="pdq-content-list">

3901<ul id="_1875">

3902<li>

3903Central review of enucleated eyes by ophthalmic pathologists identified a group of children (19% of all eligible patients) who were misclassified by the institutional pathology report.</li>

3904<li>

3905The importance of universal guidelines for handling and interpreting retinoblastoma specimens is instrumental in the recognition and categorization of high-risk features.</li>

3906<li>

3907For all patients on the study, the overall 2-year event-free survival (EFS) rate was 98%, and the overall survival (OS) rate was 99%. </li>

3908<li>

3909For patients with high-risk features requiring adjuvant chemotherapy, the 2-year EFS rate was 96%.</li>

3910<li>

3911For patients with low-risk features for whom observation was indicated, the 2-year EFS rate was 99%.</li>

3912<li>

3913The combination of extensive involvement of both the choroid and the optic nerve posterior to the lamina cribrosa represents the highest risk of recurrence and supports the need for more intensive chemotherapy for this group.</li>

3914<li>

3915The average time to first recurrence was less than 12 months, and the time to death after recurrence was approximately 8 months.</li>

3916</ul>

3917</div>

3918</section>

3919<section id="_726">

3920<h4 id="_726_toc">

3921Conservative ocular salvage approaches</h4>

3922<p id="_679" tabindex="-1">

3923Conservative ocular salvage approaches, such as systemic chemotherapy and local-control treatments, may be offered in an attempt to save the eye and preserve vision.[<a href="#cit/section_6.9">

39249</a>

3925] Ocular salvage rates correlate with intraocular grouping. While the possibility of saving the eye without the use of external-beam radiation therapy (EBRT) exceeds 80% for children with early intraocular disease, the ocular outcomes for children with advanced intraocular disease are poor using systemic chemotherapy and local treatments, with less than 40% ocular salvage rates, even after the use of EBRT.[<a href="#cit/section_6.10">

392610</a>

3927]</p>

3928<p id="_681" tabindex="-1">

3929Caution must be exerted with extended systemic chemotherapy administration and delayed enucleation when tumor control does not appear to be possible, particularly for Group E eyes. Pre-enucleation chemotherapy for eyes with advanced intraocular disease may result in downstaging and underestimate the pathological evidence of extraretinal and extraocular disease, thus increasing the risk of dissemination.[<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

393011</a>

3931]</p>

3932<p id="_682" tabindex="-1">

3933The delivery of chemotherapy via ophthalmic artery cannulation as initial treatment for advanced unilateral retinoblastoma appears to be more effective than systemic chemotherapy for chemoreduction, particularly for Group D eyes.[<a href="#cit/section_6.12">

393412</a>

3935,<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

393613</a>

3937]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.14">

393814</a>

3939][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

3940Level of evidence C3</a>

3941] In the setting of a multidisciplinary state-of-the-art center, intra-arterial chemotherapy may result in ocular salvage rates of approximately 80% to 90% for patients with advanced intraocular unilateral retinoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

394213</a>

3943-<a href="#cit/section_6.16">

394416</a>

3945] For more information, see the <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_821">

3946Ophthalmic Artery Infusion of Chemotherapy (Intra-arterial Chemotherapy)</a>

3947 section. Electroretinography, a technique that measures the electrical responses of various cell types in the retina, including the photoreceptors, can be used to assess retinal function during and after treatment. In one study, pretreatment electroretinography correlated with final visual acuity after treatment with intra-arterial chemotherapy, suggesting that this technique may potentially be used to help with treatment strategy decisions and prioritization of interventions.[<a href="#cit/section_6.17">

394817</a>

3949]</p>

3950<p id="_683" tabindex="-1">

3951Because a proportion of children who present with unilateral retinoblastoma

3952will eventually develop disease in the opposite eye, these children undergo genetic counseling and testing and periodic examinations

3953of the unaffected eye, regardless of the treatment they receive. Asynchronous bilateral disease occurs most

3954frequently in patients with affected parents and in children diagnosed during the first months of life.</p>

3955</section>

3956<section id="_1796">

3957<h4 id="_1796_toc">

3958Treatment options under clinical evaluation for unilateral intraocular retinoblastoma</h4>

3959<p id="_1817" tabindex="-1">

3960Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

3961NCI website</a>

3962. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

3963ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

3964.</p>

3965<p id="_1797" tabindex="-1">

3966The following is an example of a national and/or institutional clinical trial that is currently being conducted:</p>

3967<div class="pdq-content-list">

3968<ul id="_1798">

3969<li>

3970<strong>

3971<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT05504291">

3972ARET2121 (NCT05504291)</a>

3973</strong>

3974

3975(A Study to Give Treatment Inside the Eye to Treat Retinoblastoma)<strong>

3976:</strong>

3977 This trial is exploring the feasibility of incorporating intravitreal melphalan injections during neoadjuvant systemic chemotherapy (carboplatin, vincristine, and etoposide). Patients are eligible if they have unilateral Group D retinoblastoma with vitreous seeding or bilateral retinoblastoma with the worst eye categorized as Group D and vitreous seeding.</li>

3978</ul>

3979</div>

3990</section>

3991</section>

3992<section id="_684">

3993<h3 id="_684_toc">

3994Treatment of Bilateral Intraocular Retinoblastoma</h3>

3995<p id="_2812" tabindex="-1">

3996The goal of therapy for bilateral retinoblastoma is ocular and vision preservation and the delay or avoidance of EBRT and enucleation.</p>

3997<p id="_686" tabindex="-1">

3998Treatment options for bilateral intraocular retinoblastoma include the following:</p>

3999<div class="pdq-content-list">

4000<ol id="_687">

4001<li>

4002Enucleation for large intraocular tumors, followed by pathology-based, risk-adapted chemotherapy when the eye and vision cannot be saved.</li>

4003<li>

4004Conservative ocular salvage approaches when the eye and vision can be saved.<div class="pdq-content-list">

4005<ul id="_688">

4006<li>

4007Chemoreduction with either systemic or ophthalmic artery infusion chemotherapy with or without intravitreal chemotherapy.</li>

4008<li>

4009Local treatments, including cryotherapy, thermotherapy, and plaque radiation therapy.</li>

4010<li>

4011EBRT.</li>

4012</ul>

4013</div>

4014</li>

4015</ol>

4016</div>

4017<p id="_832" tabindex="-1">

4018Intraocular tumor burden is usually asymmetrical, and treatment is dictated by the most advanced eye.

4019 Systemic therapy is generally selected on the basis of the eye with more extensive disease. Treatment options described for unilateral disease may be applied to one or both affected eyes in patients with bilateral disease. While up-front enucleation of an advanced eye and risk-adapted adjuvant chemotherapy may be required, a more conservative approach using primary chemoreduction with close monitoring for response and aggressive local treatment is usually the treatment of choice.

4020EBRT is now reserved for patients whose eyes do not respond adequately to primary systemic or intra-arterial chemotherapy and local consolidation.[<a href="#cit/section_6.18">

402118</a>

4022]</p>

4023<p id="_690" tabindex="-1">

4024Several large centers have published trial results that used systemic chemotherapy in conjunction with aggressive local consolidation for patients with bilateral disease.[<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

402519</a>

4026] The backbone of chemoreduction has generally been carboplatin, etoposide, and vincristine. While the less toxic combination of vincristine and carboplatin can provide disease control for a significant proportion of patients, ocular salvage appears to be superior when etoposide is included in the regimen.[<a href="#cit/section_6.20">

402720</a>

4028][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810025&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4029Level of evidence B1</a>

4030] Similar results were achieved in one single-institution study when topotecan was substituted for etoposide in a combination regimen.[<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

403121</a>

4032][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4033Level of evidence C1</a>

4034] Using this approach, the International Classification of Retinoblastoma grouping system has been proven to predict ocular survival, with globe salvage rates usually exceeding 80% for Groups A and B, and 40% to 80% for Groups C and D, although EBRT may be required in more advanced intraocular cases.[<a href="#cit/section_6.22">

403522</a>

4036]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.19">

403719</a>

4038,<a href="#cit/section_6.21">

403921</a>

4040][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4041Level of evidence C1</a>

4042]</p>

4043<p id="_1842" tabindex="-1">

4044For patients with large intraocular tumor burdens with subretinal or vitreous seeds (Group D eyes), the administration of higher doses of carboplatin coupled with subtenon carboplatin, and the addition of lower doses of EBRT (36 Gy) for patients with persistent disease has been explored. Using this intensive approach, eye survival may approach a rate of 70% at 60 months.[<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

404523</a>

4046][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4047Level of evidence B4</a>

4048] </p>

4049<p id="_1843" tabindex="-1">

4050The prognosis for patients with group E eyes who are treated with systemic chemotherapy and local control measures is very poor without radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.23">

405123</a>

4052][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4053Level of evidence B4</a>

4054] The use of prolonged systemic chemotherapy for Group E eyes to avoid or delay enucleation has been associated with lower disease-specific survival.[<a href="#cit/section_6.11">

405511</a>

4056][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4057Level of evidence C1</a>

4058]</p>

4059<p id="_695" tabindex="-1">

4060Delivery of chemotherapy via ophthalmic artery cannulation with the addition of intra-vitreal chemotherapy for patients with persistent vitreous or subretinal disease has become a very strong alternative to the use of systemic chemotherapy.[<a href="#cit/section_6.12">

406112</a>

4062,<a href="#cit/section_6.13">

406313</a>

4064,<a href="#cit/section_6.15">

406515</a>

4066,<a href="#cit/section_6.16">

406716</a>

4068]; [<a href="#cit/section_6.14">

406914</a>

4070][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4071Level of evidence C3</a>

4072] While tandem administration is feasible, bilateral administrations increase the risk of systemic toxicity caused by melphalan exposure.[<a href="#cit/section_6.24">

407324</a>

4074] In these circumstances, intra-arterial chemotherapy with single-agent carboplatin may be used to treat the less-advanced eye during the tandem procedure.[<a href="#cit/section_6.25">

407525</a>

4076] These treatments should only be performed in an experienced center with a state-of-the-art treatment infrastructure and a dedicated multidisciplinary team. For more information, see the sections on <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_821">

4077Ophthalmic Artery Infusion of Chemotherapy (Intra-arterial Chemotherapy)</a>

4078 and <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_824">

4079Intravitreal Chemotherapy</a>

4080.</p>

4210<li id="section_6.21">

4211Brennan RC, Qaddoumi I, Mao S, et al.: Ocular Salvage and Vision Preservation Using a Topotecan-Based Regimen for Advanced Intraocular Retinoblastoma. J Clin Oncol 35 (1): 72-77, 2017.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=28034080&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=28034080&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4212[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4213</li>

4214<li id="section_6.22">

4215Shields CL, Mashayekhi A, Au AK, et al.: The International Classification of Retinoblastoma predicts chemoreduction success. Ophthalmology 113 (12): 2276-80, 2006.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=16996605&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=16996605&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4216[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4217</li>

4218<li id="section_6.23">

4219Berry JL, Jubran R, Kim JW, et al.: Long-term outcomes of Group D eyes in bilateral retinoblastoma patients treated with chemoreduction and low-dose IMRT salvage. Pediatr Blood Cancer 60 (4): 688-93, 2013.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=22997170&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=22997170&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4220[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4221</li>

4222<li id="section_6.24">

4223Schaiquevich P, Buitrago E, Taich P, et al.: Pharmacokinetic analysis of melphalan after superselective ophthalmic artery infusion in preclinical models and retinoblastoma patients. Invest Ophthalmol Vis Sci 53 (7): 4205-12, 2012.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=22628208&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=22628208&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4224[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4225</li>

4226<li id="section_6.25">

4227Francis JH, Gobin YP, Brodie SE, et al.: Experience of intra-arterial chemosurgery with single agent carboplatin for retinoblastoma. Br J Ophthalmol 96 (9): 1270-1, 2012.&#160;<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=22611138&amp;dopt=Abstract" title="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=Retrieve&amp;db=PubMed&amp;list_uids=22611138&amp;dopt=Abstract">

4228[PUBMED Abstract]</a>

4229</li>

4230</ol>

4231</div>

4232

4233 </section>

4234

4235

4236

4237 <section id="_77">

4238

4239 <h2>

4240Treatment of Extraocular Retinoblastoma</h2>

4241

4242 <div id="_section_77" class="pdq-sections">

4243<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4244<h6>

4245In This Section</h6>

4246<ul>

4247<li>

4248<a href="#_117_toc">

4249Treatment of Orbital and Locoregional Retinoblastoma</a>

4250</li>

4251<li>

4252<a href="#_541_toc">

4253Treatment of CNS Disease</a>

4254</li>

4255<li>

4256<a href="#_543_toc">

4257Treatment of Synchronous Trilateral Retinoblastoma</a>

4258</li>

4259<li>

4260<a href="#_546_toc">

4261Treatment of Extracranial Metastatic Retinoblastoma</a>

4262</li>

4263<li>

4264<a href="#_TrialSearch_77_sid_7_toc">

4265Current Clinical Trials</a>

4266</li>

4267</ul>

4268</nav>

4269<p id="_78" tabindex="-1">

4270 In high-income countries, few patients with retinoblastoma present with extraocular

4271disease. Extraocular disease may be localized to the soft tissues surrounding

4272the eye or to the optic nerve beyond the margin of resection. However, further

4273extension may progress into the brain and meninges, with subsequent seeding of the

4274spinal fluid and as distant metastatic disease involving the lungs, bones,

4275and bone marrow. </p>

4276<section id="_117">

4277<h3 id="_117_toc">

4278Treatment of Orbital and Locoregional Retinoblastoma</h3>

4279<p id="_540" tabindex="-1">

4280Orbital retinoblastoma occurs as a result of progression of the tumor through the emissary vessels and sclera. For this reason, transscleral disease is considered to be extraocular and should be treated as such. Orbital retinoblastoma is isolated in 60% to 70% of cases. </p>

4281<p id="_697" tabindex="-1">

4282Treatment options for extraocular retinoblastoma (orbital and locoregional) include the following:</p>

4283<div class="pdq-content-list">

4284<ol id="_698">

4285<li>

4286Chemotherapy.</li>

4287<li>

4288Enucleation (for extraocular extension).</li>

4289<li>

4290Radiation therapy.</li>

4291</ol>

4292</div>

4293<p id="_699" tabindex="-1">

4294Treatment includes systemic chemotherapy and radiation therapy. With this treatment approach, 60% to 85% of patients can be cured. Because most recurrences occur in the central nervous system (CNS), regimens that include drugs with well-documented CNS penetration are used.</p>

4295<p id="_2820" tabindex="-1">

4296The Children&#8217;s Oncology Group (COG) performed a prospective international trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00554788">

4297ARET0321 [NCT00554788]</a>

4298) that included patients with extraocular retinoblastoma. The study showed that intensified therapy improved the outcomes of patients with stage II, III or IVa disease, compared with historical controls. However, stage IVb patients need more effective therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

42991</a>

4300][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4301Level of evidence B4</a>

4302]</p>

4303<div class="pdq-content-list">

4304<ul id="_2821">

4305<li>

4306Patients with stage II or III retinoblastoma (n = 19) received four cycles of chemotherapy (vincristine, cisplatin, cyclophosphamide, etoposide) followed by involved-field radiation therapy (45 Gy). <div class="pdq-content-list">

4307<ul id="_2842">

4308<li>

4309The median follow-up was 7.3 years. </li>

4310<li>

4311The 3-year event-free survival (EFS) and overall survival (OS) rates were 88.1%.</li>

4312</ul>

4313</div>

4314</li>

4315<li>

4316Patients with stage IVa retinoblastoma (n = 18) received four cycles of induction therapy (noted above). Patients with more than a partial response underwent autologous hematopoietic stem cell support. Patients with residual tumor after chemotherapy received radiation therapy. <div class="pdq-content-list">

4317<ul id="_2843">

4318<li>

4319The 3-year EFS and OS rates were 76.7%.</li>

4320</ul>

4321</div>

4322</li>

4323<li>

4324Patients with stage IVb retinoblastoma (n = 20) received the same treatment as patients with stage IVa disease. <div class="pdq-content-list">

4325<ul id="_2844">

4326<li>

4327The 1-year EFS rate was 28.3%, and the OS rate was 42.1%.</li>

4328<li>

4329 The 3-year EFS rate was 14.2%, and the OS rate was 12.3%.</li>

4330</ul>

4331</div>

4332</li>

4333<li>

4334Six patients developed secondary malignancies. There was one case each of osteosarcoma (8 years after treatment), thyroid papillary carcinoma (9 years after treatment), glioblastoma multiforme (8 years after treatment), large B-cell Epstein-Barr virus&#8211;associated lymphoproliferative disorder (8 months after treatment), acute myeloid leukemia (2 years after treatment), and alveolar soft part sarcoma of the bladder (5 years after treatment). Five patients had unilateral retinoblastoma, two of whom received radiation therapy (one each with unilateral and bilateral).</li>

4335</ul>

4336</div>

4337<p id="_700" tabindex="-1">

4338For patients with macroscopic orbital disease, delay of surgery until response to chemotherapy is achieved (usually after receiving two or three courses of treatment) has been effective. Patients then undergo enucleation and receive an additional four to six courses of chemotherapy. Next, local control is consolidated with orbital irradiation (40&#8211;45 Gy). Using this approach, orbital exenteration is not indicated.[<a href="#cit/section_7.2">

43392</a>

4340] </p>

4341<p id="_701" tabindex="-1">

4342Patients with isolated involvement of the optic nerve at the transsection level are considered to have extraocular disease and are treated using systemic therapy, similar to that used for macroscopic orbital disease, and irradiation of the entire orbit (36 Gy) with a 10 Gy boost to the chiasm (total of 46 Gy).[<a href="#cit/section_7.3">

43433</a>

4344]</p>

4345</section>

4346<section id="_541">

4347<h3 id="_541_toc">

4348Treatment of CNS Disease</h3>

4349<p id="_542" tabindex="-1">

4350Intracranial dissemination occurs by direct extension through the optic nerve. The prognosis for these patients is dismal. Treatment includes platinum-based, intensive systemic chemotherapy and CNS-directed therapy. Although intrathecal chemotherapy has been used traditionally, there is no preclinical or clinical evidence to support its use.</p>

4351<p id="_702" tabindex="-1">

4352Treatment options for extraocular retinoblastoma (CNS disease) include the following:</p>

4353<div class="pdq-content-list">

4354<ol id="_703">

4355<li>

4356Systemic chemotherapy and CNS-directed therapy with radiation therapy.</li>

4357<li>

4358Systemic chemotherapy followed by myeloablative chemotherapy and stem cell rescue with or without radiation therapy.</li>

4359</ol>

4360</div>

4361<p id="_1869" tabindex="-1">

4362 The administration of radiation therapy to these patients is controversial. Responses have been observed with craniospinal radiation using 25 Gy to 35 Gy to the entire craniospinal axis and a boost (10 Gy) to sites of measurable disease. </p>

4363<p id="_2822" tabindex="-1">

4364The COG conducted a prospective study (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00554788">

4365ARET0321 [NCT00554788]</a>

4366) of patients with extraocular retinoblastoma, which included patients with stage IVb disease who were treated with four cycles of induction therapy (vincristine, cisplatin, cyclophosphamide, and etoposide).[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

43671</a>

4368][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4369Level of evidence B4</a>

4370] Patients who had more than a partial response were then treated with high-dose carboplatin, thiotepa, and etoposide with autologous hematopoietic stem cell support. Patients with residual disease after chemotherapy received radiation therapy. In the 20 patients with stage IVb disease, the 1-year EFS rate was 28.3%, and the OS rate was 42.1%. The 3-year EFS rate was 14.2%, and the OS rate was 12.3%.</p>

4371</section>

4372<section id="_543">

4373<h3 id="_543_toc">

4374Treatment of Synchronous Trilateral Retinoblastoma</h3>

4375<p id="_544" tabindex="-1">

4376Trilateral retinoblastoma is usually associated with a pineal lesion or, less commonly, a suprasellar lesion.[<a href="#cit/section_7.4">

43774</a>

4378-<a href="#cit/section_7.6">

43796</a>

4380] In patients with the heritable form of retinoblastoma, CNS disease is less likely the result of metastatic or regional spread than of a primary intracranial focus, such as a pineal tumor. The prognosis for patients with trilateral retinoblastoma is very poor. Most patients die of disseminated neuraxis disease in less than 9 months.[<a href="#cit/section_7.7">

43817</a>

4382,<a href="#cit/section_7.8">

43838</a>

4384] However, with increased surveillance and aggressive therapy, there has been improvement in survival, from 6% (patients treated before 1995) to 44% (patients treated after 1996).[<a href="#cit/section_7.9">

43859</a>

4386] </p>

4387<p id="_705" tabindex="-1">

4388Treatment options for synchronous trilateral retinoblastoma include the following:</p>

4389<div class="pdq-content-list">

4390<ol id="_706">

4391<li>

4392Systemic chemotherapy followed by surgery and myeloablative chemotherapy with stem cell rescue.</li>

4393<li>

4394Systemic chemotherapy followed by surgery and radiation therapy.</li>

4395</ol>

4396</div>

4397<p id="_707" tabindex="-1">

4398While pineoblastomas occurring in older patients are sensitive to radiation therapy, current strategies are directed towards avoiding radiation by using intensive chemotherapy followed by consolidation with myeloablative chemotherapy and autologous hematopoietic progenitor cell rescue, an approach similar to those being used in the treatment of brain tumors in infants.[<a href="#cit/section_7.10">

439910</a>

4400]</p>

4401<p id="_847" tabindex="-1">

4402For more information about trilateral retinoblastoma, including screening with neuroimaging, see the <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_496">

4403Trilateral retinoblastoma</a>

4404 section.</p>

4405</section>

4406<section id="_546">

4407<h3 id="_546_toc">

4408Treatment of Extracranial Metastatic Retinoblastoma</h3>

4409<p id="_709" tabindex="-1">

4410Treatment options for extracranial metastatic retinoblastoma include the following:</p>

4411<div class="pdq-content-list">

4412<ol id="_710">

4413<li>

4414Systemic chemotherapy followed by myeloablative chemotherapy with stem cell rescue and radiation therapy.</li>

4415</ol>

4416</div>

4417<p id="_2823" tabindex="-1">

4418Hematogenous metastases may develop in the bones, bone marrow, and, less frequently, the liver. The COG conducted a prospective international trial (<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT00554788">

4419ARET0321 [NCT00554788]</a>

4420) for patients with extraocular retinoblastoma. The study showed that intensified therapy improved the outcomes of patients with stage IVa disease.[<a href="#cit/section_7.1">

44211</a>

4422][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810033&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4423Level of evidence B4</a>

4424] Patients with stage IVa retinoblastoma (n = 18) were treated with four cycles of induction therapy (vincristine, cisplatin, cyclophosphamide, and etoposide). </p>

4425<div class="pdq-content-list">

4426<ul id="_2824">

4427<li>

4428The patients who had more than a partial response then received one cycle of high-dose carboplatin, thiotepa, and etoposide with autologous hematopoietic stem cell support. Patients with residual tumor after chemotherapy received radiation therapy.</li>

4429<li>

4430With a median follow up of 7.3 years, the 3-year EFS and OS rates were 76.7%.</li>

4431</ul>

4432</div>

4433</section>

4434<section id="_TrialSearch_77_sid_7">

4435<h3 id="_TrialSearch_77_sid_7_toc">

4436Current Clinical Trials</h3>

4437<p id="_TrialSearch_77_22" tabindex="-1">

4438Use our <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search/advanced">

4439advanced clinical trial search</a>

4440 to find NCI-supported cancer clinical trials that are now enrolling patients. The search can be narrowed by location of the trial, type of treatment, name of the drug, and other criteria. <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/">

4441General information</a>

4442 about clinical trials is also available.</p>

4493

4494 <section id="_85">

4495

4496 <h2>

4497Treatment of Progressive or Recurrent Retinoblastoma</h2>

4498

4499 <div id="_section_85" class="pdq-sections">

4500<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4501<h6>

4502In This Section</h6>

4503<ul>

4504<li>

4505<a href="#_848_toc">

4506Treatment of Progressive or Recurrent Intraocular Retinoblastoma</a>

4507</li>

4508<li>

4509<a href="#_849_toc">

4510Treatment of Progressive or Recurrent Extraocular Retinoblastoma</a>

4511</li>

4512<li>

4513<a href="#_2832_toc">

4514Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation for Progressive or Recurrent Retinoblastoma<ul>

4515<li>

4516<a href="#_TrialSearch_2832_sid_8_toc">

4517Current Clinical Trials</a>

4518</li>

4519</ul>

4520</a>

4521</li>

4522</ul>

4523</nav>

4524<p id="_86" tabindex="-1">

4525The prognosis for a patient with progressive or recurrent retinoblastoma

4526depends on the site and extent of the progression or recurrence and previous treatment received.</p>

4527<p id="_1915" tabindex="-1">

4528The introduction of intravenous chemotherapy for the treatment of retinoblastoma in the early 1990s revolutionized retinoblastoma management. In a retrospective review of 869 eyes in 551 patients with retinoblastoma who were treated with chemoreduction, 64% of the eyes experienced a recurrence and 94% of the recurrences or new tumors were detected within the first 3 years of treatment. Risk factors for recurrence included younger patient age at diagnosis (odds ratio [OR], 1.02 per 1 month decrease; <em>

4529P</em>

4530 = .02), more advanced International Classification of Retinoblastoma group (OR, 1.24 per 1 more advanced group; <em>

4531P</em>

4532 = .01), closer tumor distance to optic disc (OR, 1.11 per 1 mm decrease; <em>

4533P</em>

4534 = .03), and presentation with subretinal seeds (OR, 1.66; <em>

4535P</em>

4536 = .02). Germline mutations contributed to the risk of new tumor formation.[<a href="#cit/section_8.1">

45371</a>

4538][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4539Level of evidence C1</a>

4540]</p>

4541<p id="_1916" tabindex="-1">

4542Intraocular and extraocular recurrences have very different prognoses and are treated in distinctly different ways. </p>

4543<section id="_848">

4544<h3 id="_848_toc">

4545Treatment of Progressive or Recurrent Intraocular Retinoblastoma</h3>

4546<p id="_711" tabindex="-1">

4547Treatment options for progressive or recurrent intraocular retinoblastoma include the following:</p>

4548<div class="pdq-content-list">

4549<ol id="_712">

4550<li>

4551Enucleation.</li>

4552<li>

4553Radiation therapy (external-beam or plaque radiation therapy).</li>

4554<li>

4555Local treatments (cryotherapy or thermotherapy).</li>

4556<li>

4557Salvage chemotherapy (systemic or intra-arterial chemotherapy).</li>

4558<li>

4559Intravitreal chemotherapy, especially for refractory or recurrent vitreous seeding.</li>

4560</ol>

4561</div>

4562<p id="_1844" tabindex="-1">

4563For more information about the use and potential applications of intra-arterial and intravitreal chemotherapy, see the sections on <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_821">

4564Ophthalmic Artery Infusion of Chemotherapy (Intra-arterial Chemotherapy)</a>

4565 and <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_824">

4566Intravitreal Chemotherapy</a>

4567.</p>

4568<p id="_835" tabindex="-1">

4569New intraocular tumors can arise in patients with the heritable form of disease whose eyes have been treated with local control measures only because every cell in the retina carries the <em class="gene-name">

4570RB1</em>

4571 mutation. This event should not be considered a recurrence. Even with previous treatment consisting of chemoreduction and local control measures in very young patients with heritable retinoblastoma, surveillance may detect new tumors at an early stage. Additional local control therapy, including plaque radiation therapy, can successfully eradicate these tumors.[<a href="#cit/section_8.2">

45722</a>

4573]</p>

4574<p id="_836" tabindex="-1">

4575When the recurrence or progression of retinoblastoma is confined to the eye and is small, the prognosis for sight and survival may be excellent with local therapy only.[<a href="#cit/section_8.3">

45763</a>

4577][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4578Level of evidence C3</a>

4579] If the recurrence or progression is confined to the

4580eye but is extensive, the prognosis for sight is poor; however, survival

4581remains excellent.</p>

4582<p id="_1870" tabindex="-1">

4583 Intra-arterial chemotherapy (IAC) into the ophthalmic artery has been effective in patients who relapse after systemic chemotherapy and radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_8.4">

45844</a>

4585,<a href="#cit/section_8.5">

45865</a>

4587] Rescue IAC, usually with other agents, has also been used after primary IAC.[<a href="#cit/section_8.6">

45886</a>

4589] Plaque radiation therapy is an option for patients who have a retinoblastoma recurrence after IAC treatment.[<a href="#cit/section_8.7">

45907</a>

4591][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810039&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4592Level of evidence C3</a>

4593] Radiation therapy should be considered for patients who have not been previously irradiated. Finally, enucleation may be required in cases of progressive disease after all eye-salvaging treatments have failed. </p>

4594</section>

4595<section id="_849">

4596<h3 id="_849_toc">

4597Treatment of Progressive or Recurrent Extraocular Retinoblastoma</h3>

4598<p id="_744" tabindex="-1">

4599Treatment options for progressive or recurrent extraocular retinoblastoma include the following:</p>

4600<div class="pdq-content-list">

4601<ol id="_747">

4602<li>

4603Systemic chemotherapy and radiation therapy for orbital disease.</li>

4604<li>

4605Systemic chemotherapy followed by myeloablative chemotherapy with stem cell rescue and radiation therapy for extraorbital disease.</li>

4606</ol>

4607</div>

4608<p id="_1873" tabindex="-1">

4609Recurrence in the orbit after enucleation is treated with aggressive chemotherapy in addition to local radiation therapy because of the high risk of metastatic disease.[<a href="#cit/section_8.8">

46108</a>

4611][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4612Level of evidence C1</a>

4613] After enucleation for recurrence, high-resolution magnetic resonance imaging with orbital coils can be helpful in distinguishing orbital recurrence from postsurgical enhancement.[<a href="#cit/section_8.9">

46149</a>

4615]</p>

4616<p id="_715" tabindex="-1">

4617If the recurrence or progression is extraocular, the

4618chance of survival is poor.[<a href="#cit/section_8.10">

461910</a>

4620] However, the use of intensive systemic chemotherapy and consolidation with high-dose chemotherapy and autologous hematopoietic stem cell rescue may improve the chance of a cure, particularly for patients with extracranial recurrence. For patients with disease recurrence after those intensive approaches, clinical trials

4621may be considered. For more information, see the <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_77">

4622Treatment of Extraocular Retinoblastoma</a>

4623 section.</p>

4624</section>

4625<section id="_2832">

4626<h3 id="_2832_toc">

4627Treatment Options Under Clinical Evaluation for Progressive or Recurrent Retinoblastoma</h3>

4628<p id="_1845" tabindex="-1">

4629One approach under investigation for patients with progressive intraocular retinoblastoma includes the use of an oncolytic adenovirus that targets <em class="gene-name">

4630RB1</em>

4631.[<a href="#cit/section_8.11">

463211</a>

4633]</p>

4634<p id="_2833" tabindex="-1">

4635Information about National Cancer Institute (NCI)&#8211;supported clinical trials can be found on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/search">

4636NCI website</a>

4637. For information about clinical trials sponsored by other organizations, see the <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/">

4638ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4639.</p>

4640<p id="_2834" tabindex="-1">

4641The following is an example of a national and/or institutional clinical trial that is currently being conducted:</p>

4642<div class="pdq-content-list">

4643<ul id="_2835">

4644<li>

4645<strong>

4646<a href="/clinicaltrials/NCT03155620">

4647APEC1621 (NCT03155620)</a>

4648</strong>

4649 (Pediatric MATCH: Targeted Therapy Directed by Genetic Testing in Treating Pediatric Patients with Relapsed or Refractory Advanced Solid Tumors, Non-Hodgkin Lymphomas, or Histiocytic Disorders)<strong>

4650:</strong>

4651 NCI-COG Pediatric Molecular Analysis for Therapeutic Choice (MATCH), referred to as Pediatric MATCH, will match targeted agents with specific molecular changes identified in a patient's tumor (refractory or recurrent). Children and adolescents aged 1 to 21 years are eligible for the trial.<p id="_1807" tabindex="-1">

4652Patients with tumors that have molecular variants addressed by treatment arms included in the trial will be offered treatment on Pediatric MATCH. Additional information can be obtained on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/treatment/clinical-trials/nci-supported/pediatric-match">

4653NCI website</a>

4654 and <a href="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620" title="https://clinicaltrials.gov/ct2/show/NCT03155620">

4655ClinicalTrials.gov website</a>

4656.</p>

4657

4658</li>

4659</ul>

4660</div>

4725

4726 <section id="_525">

4727

4728 <h2>

4729Late Effects of Retinoblastoma Therapy</h2>

4730

4731 <div id="_section_525" class="pdq-sections">

4732<nav class="in-this-section" role="navigation">

4733<h6>

4734In This Section</h6>

4735<ul>

4736<li>

4737<a href="#_499_toc">

4738Subsequent Neoplasms (SNs)</a>

4739</li>

4740<li>

4741<a href="#_2794_toc">

4742Other Late Effects</a>

4743</li>

4744</ul>

4745</nav>

4746<p id="_807" tabindex="-1">

4747In a report from the Retinoblastoma Survivor Study (N = 470), 87% of survivors of retinoblastoma (mean age, 43 years; median follow-up, 42 years) had at least one medical condition and 71% had a severe or life-threatening condition. The adjusted relative risk of a chronic condition in survivors, compared with nonretinoblastoma controls, was 1.4 (<em>

4748P</em>

4749 &lt; .01). The relative risk of a grade 3 or 4 condition was 7.6 (<em>

4750P</em>

4751 &lt; .01). After excluding ocular conditions and subsequent neoplasms (SNs), this excess risk was found to persist only for patients with bilateral disease.[<a href="#cit/section_9.1">

47521</a>

4753]</p>

4754<section id="_499">

4755<h3 id="_499_toc">

4756Subsequent Neoplasms (SNs)</h3>

4757<p id="_653" tabindex="-1">

4758SNs are the most common cause of death in patients with retinoblastoma. SNs contribute to about 50% of deaths in patients with both bilateral disease and genetically defined heritable retinoblastoma.[<a href="#cit/section_9.2">

47592</a>

4760-<a href="#cit/section_9.4">

47614</a>

4762] Survivors of retinoblastoma have a high risk of developing SNs. </p>

4763<p id="_1857" tabindex="-1">

4764Factors that influence the risk of SNs include the following:</p>

4765<div class="pdq-content-list">

4766<ul id="_654">

4767<li>

4768<strong>

4769Heritable retinoblastoma.</strong>

4770 Patients with heritable retinoblastoma have a markedly increased

4771incidence of SNs, independent of treatment with radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_9.2">

47722</a>

4773,<a href="#cit/section_9.3">

47743</a>

4775,<a href="#cit/section_9.5">

47765</a>

4777] In a German series of 643 patients with heritable retinoblastoma, chemotherapy with or without radiation therapy was the only significant risk factor for the onset of second cancers outside the periorbital region.[<a href="#cit/section_9.3">

47783</a>

4779] A possible association between the type of <em class="gene-name">

4780RB1</em>

4781 mutation and incidence of SNs may exist, with complete loss of <em class="gene-name">

4782RB1</em>

4783 activity associated with a higher incidence of SNs.[<a href="#cit/section_9.6">

47846</a>

4785] Patients who are heterozygous for regular-penetrance <em class="gene-name">

4786RB1</em>

4787 variants may be at a higher risk of developing SNs than patients with incomplete-penetrance <em class="gene-name">

4788RB1</em>

4789 variants.[<a href="#cit/section_9.7">

47907</a>

4791] Regular penetrance refers to patients with heritable disease and classic multifocal bilateral tumors. Incomplete penetrance, while rare, is a clinical phenotype in which affected family members may not develop retinoblastoma or they present with unilateral disease. With the increase in survival of patients with heritable retinoblastoma, it has become apparent that they are also at risk of developing epithelial cancers late in adulthood. A marked increase in mortality from lung, bladder, and other epithelial cancers has been described.[<a href="#cit/section_9.8">

47928</a>

4793,<a href="#cit/section_9.9">

47949</a>

4795]<p id="_1836" tabindex="-1">

4796In a large series from two institutions, 2,053 patients with retinoblastoma (diagnosed between 1914&#8211;2016) were identified, with a maximum of 70 years of follow-up. Most deaths occurred in patients with hereditary retinoblastoma (518 of 1,129), and 267 of these deaths were caused by SNs. Increased risk of death resulting from cancers of the pancreas, large intestines, and kidney were reported. Overall risk of SNs was greater for patients who were treated with radiation therapy and chemotherapy compared with patients who were treated with radiation therapy alone, although patterns varied by organ site. In a cohort of 143 retinoblastoma survivors diagnosed between 1997 and 2006, continued improvements in mortality were seen.[<a href="#cit/section_9.4">

47974</a>

4798][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4799Level of evidence C1</a>

4800] For patients with nonhereditary retinoblastoma, only 27 deaths in 924 patients were attributed to SNs.</p>

4801 <p id="_806" tabindex="-1">

4802Among retinoblastoma survivors with heritable retinoblastoma, those with an inherited germline mutation are at a slightly higher risk of developing an SN than are those with a de novo mutation. Melanoma was the most common SN seen in patients with germline mutations.[<a href="#cit/section_9.10">

480310</a>

4804]</p>

4805</li>

4806<li>

4807<strong>

4808Past treatment for retinoblastoma with radiation therapy.</strong>

4809 The cumulative incidence of SNs was reported to be 26% (&#177; 10%) in nonirradiated patients and 58% (&#177; 10%) in irradiated patients by 50

4810years after diagnosis of retinoblastoma&#8212;a rate of about 1% per year.[<a href="#cit/section_9.11">

481111</a>

4812]<p id="_1858" tabindex="-1">

4813A German series of 633 patients with heritable retinoblastoma demonstrated a 5-year survival rate of 93%. However, 40 years later, only 80% of patients survived, with most succumbing to radiation-induced SNs (hazard ratio, approximately 3).[<a href="#cit/section_9.12">

481412</a>

4815] Other studies analyzing cohorts of patients treated with more advanced radiation planning and delivery technology have reported the SN rates to be about 9.4% in nonirradiated patients and about 30.4% in irradiated patients.[<a href="#cit/section_9.13">

481613</a>

4817]</p>

4818<p id="_1859" tabindex="-1">

4819 In a nonrandomized study that compared two contemporary cohorts of patients with hereditary retinoblastoma who were treated with either photon (n = 31) or proton (n = 55) therapy, the 10-year cumulative incidence of radiation-induced SNs was significantly different between the two groups (0% for proton radiation vs. 14% for photon radiation; <em>

4820P</em>

4821 = .015).[<a href="#cit/section_9.14">

482214</a>

4823] A longer follow-up will be required to further define the risk of SNs associated with proton radiation.</p>

4824<p id="_734" tabindex="-1">

4825The most common SN is sarcoma, specifically osteosarcoma, followed by soft tissue sarcoma and melanoma. These malignancies may occur inside or outside of the radiation field, although most are radiation induced. The carcinogenic effect of radiation therapy is associated with the dose delivered, particularly for subsequent sarcomas. A step-wise increase is apparent at all dose categories. In irradiated patients, two-thirds of SNs occur within irradiated tissue, and one-third of SNs occur outside the radiation field.[<a href="#cit/section_9.5">

48265</a>

4827,<a href="#cit/section_9.11">

482811</a>

4829,<a href="#cit/section_9.13">

483013</a>

4831,<a href="#cit/section_9.15">

483215</a>

4833]</p>

4834<p id="_1882" tabindex="-1">

4835 In a cohort of 952 irradiated survivors of hereditary retinoblastoma who were originally diagnosed between 1914 and 2006, 105 bone sarcomas and 125 soft tissue sarcomas were identified. Approximately two-thirds of these cancers occurred in the head and neck. The incidence rates were 2,000-fold higher for bone sarcomas and 500-fold higher for soft tissue sarcomas than was expected in the general population. Head and neck bone and soft tissue sarcomas were diagnosed in early childhood and continued into adulthood, with a 60-year cumulative incidence of 6.8% for bone sarcomas and 9.3% for soft tissue sarcomas. Bone and soft tissue sarcomas diagnosed elsewhere in the body were increased 169-fold and 45.7-fold, respectively, compared with the general population. Bone sarcomas primarily occurred in the long bones during adolescence. The incidence of soft tissue sarcomas was rare until age 30 years, when it rose steeply (60-year cumulative incidence, 6.6%), particularly for females (9.4%). The soft tissue sarcomas that occurred in females were leiomyosarcomas and were mainly located in the abdomen and pelvis.[<a href="#cit/section_9.5">

48365</a>

4837,<a href="#cit/section_9.11">

483811</a>

4839]; [<a href="#cit/section_9.16">

484016</a>

4841][<a href="/Common/PopUps/popDefinition.aspx?id=810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English" onclick="javascript:popWindow('defbyid','CDR0000810035&amp;version=HealthProfessional&amp;language=English'); return(false);">

4842Level of evidence C1</a>

4843]</p>

4844</li>

4845<li>

4846<strong>

4847Age at time of radiation therapy.</strong>

4848 The risk of SNs also appears to depend on the patient's age at the time that external-beam radiation therapy (EBRT) is administered, especially in children younger than 12 months. The histopathological types of SNs may be influenced by age.[<a href="#cit/section_9.13">

484913</a>

4850,<a href="#cit/section_9.17">

485117</a>

4852,<a href="#cit/section_9.18">

485318</a>

4854]</li>

4855<li>

4856<strong>

4857Previous SN.</strong>

4858 Patients who survive SNs are at a sevenfold increased risk of developing another SN.[<a href="#cit/section_9.19">

485919</a>

4860] The risk increases an additional threefold for patients treated with radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_9.20">

486120</a>

4862]</li>

4863</ul>

4864</div>

4865<p id="_840" tabindex="-1">

4866The issue of balancing long-term tumor control with the consequences of chemotherapy is unresolved. Most patients who receive chemotherapy are exposed to etoposide, which has been associated with secondary leukemia in patients without a predisposition to cancer. However, most patients are exposed at modest rates when compared with the risks associated with EBRT in heritable retinoblastoma. </p>

4867<p id="_1860" tabindex="-1">

4868Despite the known increased risk of acute myeloid leukemia (AML) associated with the use of etoposide, patients with heritable retinoblastoma are not at an increased risk of developing this SN.[<a href="#cit/section_9.21">

486921</a>

4870-<a href="#cit/section_9.23">

487123</a>

4872] An initial report conducted by informal survey methods described 15 patients who developed AML after chemotherapy. One-half of the patients also received radiation therapy.[<a href="#cit/section_9.22">

487322</a>

4874] This finding has not been substantiated by formal studies. In a single-institution study of 245 patients who received etoposide, only 1 patient developed acute promyelocytic leukemia after 79 months.[<a href="#cit/section_9.21">

487521</a>

4876] Additionally, the Surveillance, Epidemiology, and End Results (SEER) Program calculated standardized incidence rates for secondary hematopoietic malignancies in 34,867 survivors of childhood cancer. The observed-to-expected ratio of secondary AML in patients treated for retinoblastoma was zero.[<a href="#cit/section_9.24">

487724</a>

4878]</p>

4879<p id="_658" tabindex="-1">

4880Survival from SNs is certainly suboptimal and varies widely across studies.[<a href="#cit/section_9.8">

48818</a>

4882,<a href="#cit/section_9.25">

488325</a>

4884-<a href="#cit/section_9.29">

488529</a>

4886] However, with advances in therapy, it is essential that all SNs in survivors of retinoblastoma be treated with curative intent.[<a href="#cit/section_9.30">

488730</a>

4888] </p>

4889</section>

4890<section id="_2794">

4891<h3 id="_2794_toc">

4892Other Late Effects</h3>

4893<p id="_659" tabindex="-1">

4894Other late effects that may occur after treatment for retinoblastoma include the following:</p>

4895<div class="pdq-content-list">

4896<ul id="_660">

4897<li>

4898<strong>

4899Diminished orbital growth.</strong>

4900 Orbital growth is somewhat diminished after enucleation. However, the impact of enucleation on orbital volume may be minimized after placement of an orbital implant.[<a href="#cit/section_9.31">

490131</a>

4902] Asymmetry of orbital size that develops after enucleation is greater in younger children and infants, but enucleation after age 3 years does not result in orbital asymmetry.[<a href="#cit/section_9.32">

490332</a>

4904]</li>

4905<li>

4906<strong>

4907Visual-field deficits.</strong>

4908 Patients with retinoblastoma demonstrate a variety of long-term visual-field defects after treatment for their intraocular disease. These defects are related to tumor size, location, and treatment method.[<a href="#cit/section_9.33">

490933</a>

4910] <p id="_661" tabindex="-1">

4911One study of visual acuity after treatment with systemic chemotherapy and local ophthalmic therapy was conducted in 54 eyes of 40 children. After a mean follow-up of 68 months, 27 eyes (50%) had a final visual acuity of 20/40 or better, and 36 eyes (67%) had a final visual acuity of 20/200 or better. The clinical factors that predicted visual acuity of 20/40 or better were a tumor margin of at least 3 mm from the foveola and optic disc and an absence of subretinal fluid.[<a href="#cit/section_9.34">

491234</a>

4913]</p>

4914</li>

4915<li>

4916<strong>

4917

4918Strabismus.</strong>

4919 In a study of 42 survivors of retinoblastoma (64 eyes) who underwent successful conservative treatment, 69% of patients were noted to have strabismus at final follow-up (mean, 93.7 months). Exotropia was the most common type of strabismus reported. Conservative treatments included intravenous chemotherapy (62 eyes), intra-ophthalmic artery chemotherapy (10 eyes), brachytherapy (11 eyes), transpupillary thermotherapy (22 eyes), cryotherapy (47 eyes), and external-beam radiation therapy (4 eyes). On multivariate analysis, only foveal involvement was found to be a significant risk factor for developing strabismus (<em>

4920P</em>

4921 &lt; .001).[<a href="#cit/section_9.35">

492235</a>

4923] </li>

4924<li>

4925<strong>

4926Hearing loss.</strong>

4927 Because systemic carboplatin is now commonly used in the treatment of retinoblastoma, concern has been raised about hearing loss related to therapy. For more information, see the sections on <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_740">

4928Treatment of Intraocular Retinoblastoma</a>

4929 and <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_77">

4930Treatment of Extraocular Retinoblastoma</a>

4931.<p id="_1861" tabindex="-1">

4932 While two large studies that included children treated with six cycles of carboplatin-containing therapy (18.6 mg/kg per cycle) showed an incidence of treatment-related hearing loss of lower than 1%,[<a href="#cit/section_9.36">

493336</a>

4934,<a href="#cit/section_9.37">

493537</a>

4936] a separate series documented some degree of hearing loss in 17% of patients.[<a href="#cit/section_9.38">

493738</a>

4938] In the latter study, younger age (younger than 6 months at the time of treatment) and higher carboplatin systemic exposures correlated with an increased risk of ototoxicity.[<a href="#cit/section_9.38">

493938</a>

4940,<a href="#cit/section_9.39">

494139</a>

4942]</p>

4943</li>

4944<li>

4945<strong>

4946Neurocognitive functioning.</strong>

4947 Early studies of intellectual functioning in survivors of retinoblastoma suggested above average intelligence among survivors of bilateral disease compared with unaffected siblings and the general population, especially in those who were blind as a result of their disease.[<a href="#cit/section_9.40">

494840</a>

4949-<a href="#cit/section_9.42">

495042</a>

4951] <p id="_2795" tabindex="-1">

4952Later studies have yielded mixed results with conflicting findings, in part, resulting from the low test-retest reliability of measures used to assess cognitive outcomes at a very young age, as well as temporal differences in treatment exposures.</p>

4953<div class="pdq-content-list">

4954<ul id="_2496" class="list-dash">

4955<li>

4956Serial assessment of cognitive and adaptive functioning in a group of survivors younger than 6 years revealed declines in developmental functioning over time, with the most pronounced declines observed in patients with 13q deletions.[<a href="#cit/section_9.43">

495743</a>

4958]<div class="pdq-content-list">

4959<ul id="_2793">

4960<li>

4961Further longitudinal follow-up of this cohort identified improvement in adaptive functioning in all treatment groups and improvement in cognitive functioning in survivors who were treated with enucleation alone from the age of 5 years to the age of 10 years.[<a href="#cit/section_9.44">

496244</a>

4963]</li>

4964<li>

4965At age 10 years, overall functioning was generally within the average range, although estimated intelligence quotient was significantly below the normative mean for children who were treated with enucleation alone.[<a href="#cit/section_9.44">

496644</a>

4967]</li>

4968</ul>

4969</div>

4970</li>

4971<li>

4972A study of very long-term adult survivors of retinoblastoma, who were on average 33 years postdiagnosis, demonstrated largely average cognitive functioning across domains of intelligence, memory, attention, and executive function.[<a href="#cit/section_9.45">

497345</a>

4974]</li>

4975</ul>

4976</div>

4977</li>

4978</ul>

4979</div>

4980</section>

4981<p id="_784" tabindex="-1">

4982For specific information about the incidence, type, and monitoring of late effects in childhood and adolescent cancer survivors, see <a href="/types/childhood-cancers/late-effects-hp-pdq">

4983Late Effects of Treatment for Childhood Cancer</a>

4984.</p>

5175 <section id="_131">

5176

5177 <h2>

5178Changes to This Summary (04/11/2023)</h2>

5179

5180 <div id="_section_131" class="pdq-sections">

5181<p id="_147" tabindex="-1">

5182The PDQ cancer information summaries are reviewed regularly and updated as

5183new information becomes available. This section describes the latest

5184changes made to this summary as of the date above.</p>

5185<p id="_2846" tabindex="-1">

5186<strong>

5187<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_15">

5188Staging and Grouping Systems for Retinoblastoma</a>

5189</strong>

5190</p>

5191<p id="_2847" tabindex="-1">

5192Added <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_2836">

5193Table 3</a>

5194 about the clinical primary tumor definitions of the American Joint Committee on Cancer (AJCC) staging system.</p>

5195<p id="_2848" tabindex="-1">

5196Added <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_2837">

5197Table 5</a>

5198 about the clinical regional lymph node definitions of the AJCC staging system.</p>

5199<p id="_2849" tabindex="-1">

5200Added <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_2838">

5201Table 9</a>

5202 about the AJCC prognostic stage groups for clinical stages.</p>

5203<p id="_2850" tabindex="-1">

5204<strong>

5205<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_841">

5206Special Considerations for the Treatment of Children With Cancer</a>

5207</strong>

5208</p>

5209<p id="_2851" tabindex="-1">

5210Revised <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_845">

5211text</a>

5212 to state that between 1975 and 2020, childhood cancer mortality decreased by more than 50% (cited National Cancer Institute as reference 6 and Surveillance Research Program, National Cancer Institute as reference 7).</p>

5213<p id="_2845" tabindex="-1">

5214<strong>

5215<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_740">

5216Treatment of Intraocular Retinoblastoma</a>

5217</strong>

5218</p>

5219<p id="_2852" tabindex="-1">

5220Added <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1796">

5221Treatment options under clinical evaluation for unilateral intraocular retinoblastoma</a>

5222 as a new subsection.</p>

5223<p id="_2853" tabindex="-1">

5224Added <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_2828">

5225Treatment options under clinical evaluation for bilateral intraocular retinoblastoma</a>

5226 as a new subsection.</p>

5227<p id="_2854" tabindex="-1">

5228<strong>

5229<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_77">

5230Treatment of Extraocular Retinoblastoma</a>

5231</strong>

5232</p>

5233<p id="_2855" tabindex="-1">

5234Added <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_2820">

5235text</a>

5236 about a Children&#8217;s Oncology Group (COG) prospective international trial, which showed that intensified therapy improved the outcomes of patients with stage II, III, or IVA extraocular retinoblastoma, compared with historical controls (cited Dunkel et al. as reference 1 and level of evidence B4).</p>

5237<p id="_2856" tabindex="-1">

5238Added <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_2822">

5239text</a>

5240 about the results of a COG prospective study of patients with extraocular retinoblastoma that included patients with stage IVb disease who were treated with four cycles of induction therapy.</p>

5241<p id="_2857" tabindex="-1">

5242Added <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_2823">

5243text</a>

5244 about a COG prospective international trial, which showed that intensified therapy improved the outcomes of patients with stage IVA extraocular retinoblastoma, compared with historical controls.</p>

5245<p id="_2858" tabindex="-1">

5246<strong>

5247<a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_85">

5248Treatment of Progressive or Recurrent Retinoblastoma</a>

5249</strong>

5250</p>

5251<p id="_2859" tabindex="-1">

5252Added <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_1870">

5253text</a>

5254 to state that plaque radiation therapy is an option for patients who have a retinoblastoma recurrence after intra-arterial chemotherapy (cited Ruben et al. as reference 7 and level of evidence C3).</p>

5255<p id="_disclaimerHP_3" tabindex="-1">

5256This summary is written and maintained by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment">

5257PDQ Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board</a>

5258, which is

5259editorially independent of NCI. The summary reflects an independent review of

5260the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or NIH. More

5261information about summary policies and the role of the PDQ Editorial Boards in

5262maintaining the PDQ summaries can be found on the <a href="/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq#_AboutThis_1">

5263About This PDQ Summary</a>

5264 and <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq">

5265PDQ&#174; Cancer Information for Health Professionals</a>

5266 pages.

5267</p>

5273

5274 <section id="_AboutThis_1">

5275

5276 <h2>

5277About This PDQ Summary</h2>

5278

5279 <div id="_section_AboutThis_1" class="pdq-sections">

5280<section id="_AboutThis_2">

5281<h3 id="_AboutThis_2_toc">

5282Purpose of This Summary</h3>

5283<p id="_AboutThis_3" tabindex="-1">

5284This PDQ cancer information summary for health professionals provides comprehensive, peer-reviewed, evidence-based information about the treatment of retinoblastoma. It is intended as a resource to inform and assist clinicians in the care of their patients. It does not provide formal guidelines or recommendations for making health care decisions.</p>

5285</section>

5286<section id="_AboutThis_4">

5287<h3 id="_AboutThis_4_toc">

5288Reviewers and Updates</h3>

5289<p id="_AboutThis_5" tabindex="-1">

5290This summary is reviewed regularly and updated as necessary by the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment" title="https://www.cancer.gov/publications/pdq/editorial-boards/pediatric-treatment">

5291PDQ Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board</a>

5292, which is editorially independent of the National Cancer Institute (NCI). The summary reflects an independent review of the literature and does not represent a policy statement of NCI or the National Institutes of Health (NIH).</p>

5293<p id="_AboutThis_22" tabindex="-1">

5294 Board members review recently published articles each month to determine whether an article should:</p>

5295<div class="pdq-content-list">

5296<ul id="_AboutThis_6">

5297<li>

5298be discussed at a meeting,</li>

5299<li>

5300be cited with text, or</li>

5301<li>

5302replace or update an existing article that is already cited.</li>

5303</ul>

5304</div>

5305<p id="_AboutThis_7" tabindex="-1">

5306Changes to the summaries are made through a consensus process in which Board members evaluate the strength of the evidence in the published articles and determine how the article should be included in the summary.</p>

5307<p id="" tabindex="-1">

5308The lead reviewers for Retinoblastoma Treatment are:</p>

5309<div class="pdq-content-list">

5310<ul id="">

5311<li>

5312Karen J. Marcus, MD, FACR (Dana-Farber Cancer Institute/Boston Children's Hospital)</li>

5313<li>

5314Thomas A. Olson, MD (Aflac Cancer and Blood Disorders Center of Children's Healthcare of Atlanta - Egleston Campus)</li>

5315<li>

5316D. Williams Parsons, MD, PhD (Texas Children's Hospital)</li>

5317<li>

5318Carlos Rodriguez-Galindo, MD (St. Jude Children's Research Hospital)</li>

5319<li>

5320Nita Louise Seibel, MD (National Cancer Institute)</li>

5321</ul>

5322</div>

5323<p id="_AboutThis_9" tabindex="-1">

5324Any comments or questions about the summary content should be submitted to Cancer.gov through the NCI website's <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us" title="https://www.cancer.gov/contact/email-us">

5325Email Us</a>

5326. Do not contact the individual Board Members with questions or comments about the summaries. Board members will not respond to individual inquiries.</p>

5327</section>

5328<section id="_AboutThis_10">

5329<h3 id="_AboutThis_10_toc">

5330Levels of Evidence</h3>

5331<p id="_AboutThis_11" tabindex="-1">

5332Some of the reference citations in this summary are accompanied by a level-of-evidence designation. These designations are intended to help readers assess the strength of the evidence supporting the use of specific interventions or approaches. The PDQ Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board uses a <a href="/publications/pdq/levels-evidence/treatment">

5333formal evidence ranking system</a>

5334 in developing its level-of-evidence designations.</p>

5335</section>

5336<section id="_AboutThis_12">

5337<h3 id="_AboutThis_12_toc">

5338Permission to Use This Summary</h3>

5339<p id="_AboutThis_13" tabindex="-1">

5340PDQ is a registered trademark. Although the content of PDQ documents can be used freely as text, it cannot be identified as an NCI PDQ cancer information summary unless it is presented in its entirety and is regularly updated. However, an author would be permitted to write a sentence such as &#8220;NCI&#8217;s PDQ cancer information summary about breast cancer prevention states the risks succinctly: [include excerpt from the summary].&#8221;</p>

5341<p id="_AboutThis_14" tabindex="-1">

5342The preferred citation for this PDQ summary is:</p>

5343<p id="_AboutThis_15" tabindex="-1">

5344PDQ&#174; Pediatric Treatment Editorial Board. PDQ Retinoblastoma Treatment. Bethesda, MD: National Cancer Institute. Updated &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. Available at: <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq" title="https://www.cancer.gov/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq">

5345https://www.cancer.gov/types/retinoblastoma/hp/retinoblastoma-treatment-pdq</a>

5346. Accessed &lt;MM/DD/YYYY&gt;. [PMID: 26389442]</p>

5347<p id="_AboutThis_16" tabindex="-1">

5348Images in this summary are used with permission of the author(s), artist, and/or publisher for use within the PDQ summaries only. Permission to use images outside the context of PDQ information must be obtained from the owner(s) and cannot be granted by the National Cancer Institute. Information about using the illustrations in this summary, along with many other cancer-related images, is available in <a href="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/" title="https://visualsonline.cancer.gov/">

5349Visuals Online</a>

5350, a collection of over 2,000 scientific images.

5355<h3 id="_AboutThis_17_toc">

5356Disclaimer</h3>

5357<p id="_AboutThis_18" tabindex="-1">

5358Based on the strength of the available evidence, treatment options may be described as either &#8220;standard&#8221; or &#8220;under clinical evaluation.&#8221; These classifications should not be used as a basis for insurance reimbursement determinations. More information on insurance coverage is available on Cancer.gov on the <a href="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care" title="https://www.cancer.gov/about-cancer/managing-care">

5359Managing Cancer Care</a>

5360 page.</p>
